Under this parametrization, the semicircle in Figure 3.1 can be thought of as a
map from [0, Ï€ ] to the complex plane, as in Figure 3.2.
Definition 3.1.1. A parametric form of a curve is a representation of the curve by
a pair of equations x = x(t) and y = y(t), where t ranges over a set of real numbers,
usually a closed interval [a, b]. Each value of t determines a point Î³ (t) = (x(t), y(t)),
which traces the curve as t moves from a to b; see Figure 3.3.

Fig. 3.3 Î³ (t) traces the curve as t moves from t0 to t5 .

Let Î³ (t), a â‰¤ t â‰¤ b, be a parametrization of a curve. As t varies from a to b the
point Î³ (t) traces the curve in a specific direction, starting with Î³ (a), the initial point
of Î³ , and ending at Î³ (b), the terminal point of Î³ . This direction is usually denoted
by an arrow on the curve. A curve is closed if Î³ (a) = Î³ (b). For circles and circular
arcs, if the arrow points in the counterclockwise direction, we say that the curve
has positive orientation. Curves traversed in the clockwise direction have negative
orientation (see Figure 3.4).
It is a bit of a challenge to provide an exact definition of the notion of orientation
for general closed curves and we omit this, relying on the intuition provided by our
understanding of the movement of the hands of a clock in concrete examples.
A curve may have more than one parametrization. For example, the interval [0, 1]
can be parametrized as Î³1 (t) = t, 0 â‰¤ t â‰¤ 1 or Î³2 (t) = t 2 , 0 â‰¤ t â‰¤ 1. Both Î³1 and
Î³2 represent the same curve. In our analysis, we always choose and work with a
specific parametrization of the curve. For that reason, it will be convenient to refer
to a curve by its parametrization Î³ (t) or simply Î³ , even though it may have more
than one parametrization.

The curves in the preceding examples are all continuous, as can be seen from

their differentiability, we investigate the derivative of a complex-valued function of
a real variable. This is unrelated to the derivative of a complex-valued function of a
complex variable.

Not all properties of the derivative of a real-valued function hold for complexvalued functions. Most notably, the mean value property fails for complex-valued
functions. For real-variable functions, the mean value property states that if f is
continuous on [a, b] and differentiable on (a, b), then f (b) âˆ’ f (a) = f  (c)(b âˆ’ a)
for some c in (a, b).
This property does not hold for
complex-valued functions. To see this,
consider f (x) = eix for x in [0, 2Ï€ ]. Then f
is continuous on [0, 2Ï€ ] and has a derivative f  (x) = i eix on (0, 2Ï€ ). Also,
f (2Ï€ ) âˆ’ f (0) = 1 âˆ’ 1 = 0,
but f  never vanishes since
| f  (x)| = |i eix | = |eix | = 1.
Hence there is no number c in (0, 2Ï€ )
such that f (2Ï€ ) âˆ’ f (0) = (2Ï€ âˆ’ 0) f  (c),
and so the mean value property does
not hold for complex-valued functions
(Figure 3.9).

Fig. 3.9 The mean value property fails for complex-valued
functions of a real variable.

Paths (Contours)
We introduce the notion of a path, which is fundamental in the theory of complex
integration. Recall that a curve already contains intrinsically the notion of continuity.
We now attach to it the fundamental analytical property of differentiability.
A continuous map f from [a, b] to C is called continuously differentiable if
it is also differentiable on [a, b] and its derivative f  is continuous on [a, b]. The
f (a)
derivative f  is defined at a and b as the one-sided limits f  (a) = limtâ†“a f (t)âˆ’
tâˆ’a
f (b)
and f  (b) = limtâ†‘b f (t)âˆ’
. We extend this notion by allowing Î³ to have one-sided
tâˆ’b
derivatives at finitely many points in (a, b).

Definition 3.1.9. A continuous complex-valued function f defined on a closed
interval [a, b] is called piecewise continuously differentiable if there exist points
a1 < a2 < Â· Â· Â· < amâˆ’1 in (a, b) such that f  is continuously differentiable on each
interval [a j , a j+1 ] for j = 0, 1, . . . , m âˆ’ 1, where a0 = a and am = b. In other words:
(i) f  (t) exists for all t in (a j , a j+1 ) and at the endpoints a j , a j+1 as one-sided limit.
(ii) f  is continuous on each interval [a jâˆ’1 , a j ] for j = 1, . . . , m.
Note that if f  is defined on [a, b], it may have jump discontinuities at some a j .

146

3 Complex Integration

Definition 3.1.10. A path or a contour is a curve Î³ defined a closed interval [a, b]
which is continuously differentiable or piecewise continuously differentiable. The
path Î³ is closed if Î³ (a) = Î³ (b).
Definition 3.1.11. Given points a0 < a1 < Â· Â· Â· < am and paths Î³ j on [a jâˆ’1 , a j ], j =
1, . . . , m, such that Î³ j (a j ) = Î³ j+1 (a j ) for all j = 1, . . . , m âˆ’ 1, the combined path

Î“ = [Î³1 , . . . , Î³m ]
is piecewise defined on [a0 , am ] by Î“ (t) = Î³ j (t) for t âˆˆ [a jâˆ’1 , a j ], j = 1, . . . , m.
Thus, according to Definitions 3.1.10 and 3.1.11, a path or a contour Î³ is a finite
sequence of continuously differentiable curves, Î³1 , Î³2 , . . . , Î³m , joined at the endpoints, i.e., Î³ = [Î³1 , . . . , Î³m ]. The path Î³ is closed if the initial point of Î³1 coincides
the terminal point of Î³m , i.e., Î³1 (a0 ) = Î³m (am ).

Example 3.1.13. (Polygonal paths) A polygonal path Î³ = [z1 , z2 , . . . , zn ] is the

A polygonal path is called simple if it does

the endpoints, that is, z1 and zn may coincide.
The polygonal path is called closed if z1 = zn .

Example 3.1.14. (Degenerate and doubly traced paths)
(a) Describe the path of the curve Î³ (t) = z0 , a â‰¤ t â‰¤ b.
(b) Describe the path of Î³2 (t) = Reit (R > 0), 0 â‰¤ t â‰¤ 4Ï€ , and discuss how it is
different from that of the curve Î³1 (t) = Reit , 0 â‰¤ t â‰¤ 2Ï€ .
Solution. (a) As t ranges through the interval [a, b], the value of Î³ (t) remains fixed
at the point z0 . Clearly Î³ is continuous and Î³  = 0 is also continuous, so Î³ is a path,
which has degenerated to a single point (Figure 3.12).

Fig. 3.12 A path that degenerates to a point.

Fig. 3.13 A doubly traced circle.

(b) Points on the path Î³2 are on the circle of radius R, centered at the origin. As
t ranges from 0 to 4Ï€ , Î³2 (t) goes around the circle twice. This path is shown in
Figure 3.13. One cannot automatically tell from the picture that the path is traced
twice and so we use double arrows to precisely indicate this fact, i.e., that the path
Î³2 is traced twice in the indicated counterclockwise direction. The path Î³1 traces
around the circle only once. The two paths have the same graph but Î³2 has double

the length of Î³1 .
As a convention, whenever we refer to a closed path, we mean the path that is
traversed only once, unless stated otherwise.

We begin by extending the definition of piecewise continuous functions to complexvalued functions.
Definition 3.2.1. A complex-valued function f defined on a closed interval [a, b] is
said to be piecewise continuous if there exist points a1 < a2 < Â· Â· Â· < amâˆ’1 in (a, b)

(i) f is continuous at every point in [a, b] \ {a1 , a2 , . . . , amâˆ’1 }.
(ii) The limits lim f (t) and lim f (t) exist and are finite for every j = 1, . . . , m âˆ’ 1.

Antiderivatives of Complex-Valued Functions
Definition 3.2.5. If f is a piecewise continuous complex-valued function on [a, b],
we say that F is an antiderivative of f if F  = f at all the points of continuity of f
on (a, b). Hence if f = u + i v and F = U + iV , with u, v,U,V real-valued, then the
equalities U  (x) = u(x) and V  (x) = v(x) hold for all but finitely many x in (a, b).
We say that F is a continuous antiderivative of f , if F is an antiderivative of f and
it is a continuous function.

Fig. 3.18 An antiderivative of
a piecewise continuous function
may not be continuous.

Fig. 3.19 Selecting a continuous
antiderivative.

Using the previous notation, we write [a, b] as the finite union of adjacent closed
subintervals [a0 , a1 ], [a1 , a2 ], . . ., [amâˆ’1 , am ], with a0 = a and am = b, and such that
f is continuous on each subinterval. The functions U and V are continuous on each
subinterval and this makes F piecewise continuous on [a, b]. In general, F may not

3.2 Complex Integration

153

be continuous at the points a j (see Figure 3.18); but, as we show momentarily, a
continuous antiderivative can always be found.
Let f j denote the restriction of f to [a jâˆ’1 , a j ], and let Fj denote an antiderivative
of f j over [a jâˆ’1 , a j ]. Each Fj is computed up to an arbitrary complex constant, which
can be determined in such a way to make F continuous.
Start by setting the arbitrary constant in F1 equal 0. Then determine the constant in F2 so that limxâ†‘a1 F1 (x) = limxâ†“a1 F2 (x). (We use the up-arrow to denote
a limit from the left and the down-arrow a limit from the right.) This determines
F2 and makes the antiderivative of f continuous on [a, a2 ]. Continue in this fashion: Once we have found Fj , determine the constant in Fj+1 so that limxâ†‘a j Fj (x) =
limxâ†“a j Fj+1 (x). By construction, the resulting function F will be continuous on
[a, b] (see Figure 3.19). The following example illustrates the method.

Under what conditions is the path integral independent of the path? We address
this important question in the next section. Another question that comes to mind
as we work with path integrals concerns the parametrization of the path. Since a
path may be parametrized in many different ways, is the integral independent of the
choice of the parametrization?
Example 3.2.15. The positively oriented unit circle C1 (0) can be parametrized by
Î³1 (s) = eis , 0 â‰¤ s â‰¤ 2Ï€ or Î³2 (t) = e2Ï€ it , 0 â‰¤ t â‰¤ 1. If f is a continuous function on

The same proof works in general, but we have to explain what we mean by two
parametrizations being the same. To do so, we need to recall the notion of a continuously differentiable map. A map from [a, b] to R is called continuously differentiable
if it is continuous on [a, b] and has a continuous derivative on (a, b).
Definition 3.2.16. We say that the paths Î³1 (t), a â‰¤ t â‰¤ b, and Î³2 (s), c â‰¤ s â‰¤ d, have
equivalent parametrizations if there is a strictly increasing continuously differentiable function Ï† from [c, d] onto [a, b] such that Ï† (c) = a and Ï† (d) = b and
Î³2 (s) = Î³1 â—¦ Ï† (s) for all s in [c, d], or equivalently Î³1 (t) = Î³2 â—¦ Ï† âˆ’1 (t) for all t âˆˆ [a, b],
where Ï† âˆ’1 is the unique inverse of Ï† .
Notice that the range of paths with equivalent parametrizations is the same. Moreover, the orientation of the trajectories of both paths is the same. Using this terminology, the two parametrizations of Example 3.2.15 are equivalent.
The next result confirms a property that we would expect from a path integral.

3.3 Independence of Paths
In calculus, the problem of computing a definite integral over an interval was
often reduced to finding an antiderivative and evaluating it at the endpoints. This
is achieved by the fundamental theorem of calculus which says that if g is a continuous function on an interval [a, b] and G is an antiderivative of g on (a, b), then
b
a

g(t) dt = G(b) âˆ’ G(a).

In this section, we investigate an analogous way to compute path integrals.
Definition 3.3.1. Let f be a continuous function on a region Î© . We say that F is
an antiderivative of f in Î© if F is an analytic function on Î© with the property
F  (z) = f (z) for all z in Î© .
Since f is continuous, F is analytic and, in particular, it is continuous. Moreover,
any two antiderivatives of f differ by a constant, as a consequence of Theorem 2.5.7.
To find an antiderivative of a continuous function f we try, if possible, to guess
our answer based on formulas from calculus. For example, if f (z) = z ez , we guess
the antiderivative F(z) = z ez âˆ’ ez . Then we can verify this guess by differentiation.
As the following examples illustrate, part of checking the answer is to make sure
that the antiderivative is analytic on the domain of definition.

168

3 Complex Integration

(b) The function f (z) = Log z is continuous for z in C \ (âˆ’âˆž, 0]. An antiderivative
is F(z) = z Log z âˆ’ z, and the identity F  (z) = f (z) holds for all z in C \ (âˆ’âˆž, 0].
(c) The function f (z) = 1z is continuous in C \ {0}. We may guess an antiderivative

is an antiderivative of 1z in C \ (âˆ’âˆž, 0] only, even though f is continuous in C \ {0}.

While Theorem 3.3.4 is very useful, it has limited applications when it comes
to the evaluation of general integrals. For example, while it is immediate that the
integral of ez over a closed path is zero (ez is an antiderivative of itself), it is not
2
clear whether the same is true for the function ez , since we do not know whether it
has an antiderivative. It is desirable to have a result that would enable us to compute
integrals of analytic functions over closed paths without resorting to antiderivatives.
In the next section we prove one such result, called Cauchyâ€™s theorem; this is at the
heart of the theory of path integrals and indeed all of complex analysis.

3.4 Cauchyâ€™s Integral Theorem for Simple Paths
In this section, we prove Cauchyâ€™s theorem for analytic functions, assuming continuity of their derivatives. This version is not the most general available but is sufficient for concrete applications where the derivative is explicit. Versions of this result
without assuming continuity of derivatives can be found in subsequent sections.

Simple Curves and Greenâ€™s Theorem
Definition 3.4.1. A curve is called simple if it does not intersect itself except possibly at the endpoints. In other words, if Î³ is parametrized by [a, b] and a â‰¤ t1 < t2 â‰¤ b,
then Î³ is a simple curve if Î³ (t1 ) = Î³ (t2 ) whenever t1 = t2 . A simple closed curve
is both simple and closed; i.e., it intersects itself only at the endpoints; that is, if
a â‰¤ t1 < t2 â‰¤ b, then Î³ (t1 ) = Î³ (t2 ) if and only if t1 = a and t2 = b. A simple curve
that is also a path (see Definition 3.1.10) is called a simple path.
A simple closed curve can loop around and look very complicated, but it cannot
cross itself. A simple closed curve is also called a Jordan curve, after the French
mathematician Camille Jordan (1838â€“1922), who showed that a simple closed curve
Î³ divides the plane into two regions: a bounded region, called the interior of Î³ , and
an unbounded region, called the exterior of Î³ .
In Figure 3.34 the region D is the interior and U is the exterior. This is the
famous Jordan curve theorem from
topology, which is easy to picture but
quite difficult to prove. A proof can be
found in The Jordan curve theorem,
formally and informally, Amer. Math.
Monthly 114 (10), (2007), 882â€“894,
by Thomas C. Hales.

Fig. 3.34 A Jordan path Î³ and
the interior domain D and the
exterior domain U.

Since the interior and the exterior regions relative to a simple closed curve are
identifiable, we may define positive and negative orientations of such a curve. The
positive orientation of a simple closed curve is the one that places the interior
region to our left when traced; see Figure 3.35. The negative orientation is the
one that, when traced, places the interior region to our right; see Figure 3.36. Our
definition of positive versus negative orientation thus generalizes our earlier idea of
counterclockwise versus clockwise orientation of circles.

178

Fig. 3.36 A curve Î³ with negative
orientation inside a region Î© .

3.5 The Cauchy-Goursat Theorem
Our next goal is to prove Cauchyâ€™s theorem without assuming that the derivative of
the analytic function is continuous. This requires an effort and occupies this and the
next section. In this section we prove this theorem only for special kinds of sets,
called star-shaped.
The fact that an analytic function has a complex derivative at a point means that
near this point it can be approximated by a linear function. Since linear functions
have antiderivatives and thus vanishing integral over closed paths, it follows that
the integral of the analytic function over every closed triangle is small. This argument can be made precise and is the foundation of the proof of the general form of
Cauchyâ€™s theorem. By a closed triangle we mean the interior and the perimeter of a
triangle. For a closed triangle  we set Diam() to be the diameter of a triangle,

Cauchyâ€™s Theorem for Star-shaped Domains
A subset V of the complex plane C is called convex if for all a, b âˆˆ V we have
(1 âˆ’ t)a + tb âˆˆ V

for all t âˆˆ [0, 1].

Geometrically speaking, convex sets contain all closed line segments whose endpoints lie in the set. For instance, interiors of open or closed squares and balls are
convex sets. The notion of star-shaped sets extends that of convex sets.
Definition 3.5.3. A subset V of the complex plane C is called star-shaped about a
point z0 in V if for all z âˆˆ V we have
(1 âˆ’ t)z0 + tz âˆˆ V

for all t âˆˆ [0, 1].

3.5 The Cauchy-Goursat Theorem

185

In other words, star-shaped sets contain all closed line segments starting from a
fixed point z0 and ending at an arbitrary point in the set. See Figure 3.39. Convex
sets are always star-shaped about any point in them, but the converse is not true.

Fig. 3.39 These non-convex regions are star-shaped regions about the points indicated by dots.

3.6 Cauchy Integral Theorem For Simply Connected Regions
In this section we extend Cauchyâ€™s theorem from star-shaped sets to general regions.
The approach we take is based on the geometric concept of deformation of paths
that is easy to visualize, as illustrated by many examples. A precise definition is
motivated by the following three cases:
â€¢ Suppose that Î± and Î² are points in a region Î© , and Î³0 and Î³1 are paths in Î©
joining Î± to Î² . This means that the initial point of both Î³0 and Î³1 is Î± while the
terminal point of both Î³0 and Î³1 is Î² . It may be possible to move Î³0 continuously
so that it coincides with Î³1 , keeping the ends fixed at Î± and Î² and without leaving
Î© (Figure 3.40).
â€¢ If Î³0 and Î³1 are two closed paths in a region Î© , it may be possible to continuously
move Î³0 without leaving Î© , in such a way that it coincides with Î³1 in position
and direction (Figure 3.41).
â€¢ If Î³0 is a closed path in a region Î© , it may be the case that we can continuously
shrink Î³0 to a point z0 in Î© without leaving Î© (Figure 3.42). This situation is a
special case of the preceding one, when the Î³1 (t) = z0 for all t, i.e., it is degenerated into a point.

Fig. 3.40 Continuous deforma- Fig. 3.41 Continuous deforma- Fig. 3.42 Continuous deformation of Î³0 into Î³1 .
tion of Î³0 into Î³1 .
tion of Î³0 into a point z0 .

Definition 3.6.1. Let Î³0 , Î³1 be paths in a region Î© , both defined on [0, 1]. Assume
that Î³0 (0) = Î³1 (0) = Î± âˆˆ Î© and Î³0 (1) = Î³1 (1) = Î² âˆˆ Î© . We say that Î³0 is homotopic
to Î³1 (relative to Î© ) if there exists a continuous map H from the unit square Q =
[0, 1] Ã— [0, 1] to Î© with the following properties:
H(t, 0) = Î³0 (t),
H(t, 1) = Î³1 (t),

The continuous mapping H is called a homotopy or a continuous deformation.

the image of the lower side of Q is Î³0 . Condition (3.6.2) says that the image of the
upper side of Q is Î³1 . Conditions (3.6.3) and (3.6.4) dictate that the vertical sides of
Q are mapped to the endpoints of Î³0 and Î³1 .
The fact that Q is mapped into
Î© asserts that the image of any

in Î© and because the vertical
sides of Q are mapped to the endpoints of Î³0 and Î³1 , this curve has
the same endpoints as Î³0 and Î³1 .
As s varies from 0 to 1, the curve

the square Q varies continuously
between the two extreme curves,
from Î³0 to Î³1 . For this reason a
homotopy is also called a continFig. 3.43 Continuous deformation
uous deformation.
The definition of a continuous deformation for closed paths is similar, but there
is an additional requirement that all intermediate paths be closed.
Definition 3.6.2. Let Î³0 , Î³1 be closed paths in a region Î© , both defined on [0, 1]. We
say that Î³0 is homotopic to Î³1 (relative to Î© ) if there exists a continuous map H

Simply Connected Regions
It is important to be precise about the region Î© when talking about deformation
of paths. For instance, consider the situations depicted in Figures 3.57 and 3.58. In
Figure 3.57 the upper and lower semicircles joining the points âˆ’1 to 1 are homotopic
in the disk of radius 2. However, in Figure 3.58, the upper and lower semicircles
joining the points âˆ’1 to 1 are not homotopic in the punctured disk of radius 2. You
can imagine a stick protruding at the origin, which will prevent us from continuously
deforming the upper part of the unit circle onto the lower one.

Fig. 3.57 Continuous deformation is possible.

Fig. 3.58 Continuous deformation is not possible.

The situation encountered in the preceding examples leads to the following
important definition.
Definition 3.6.6. A region Î© is called simply connected if all paths joining Î± to
Î² in Î© are homotopic relative to Î© . If a region is not simply connected, then it is
called multiply connected.
Geometrically, a region is simply connected if and only if it has no holes in it.
The regions in Figures 3.59 are simply connected but the ones in 3.60 are not simply
connected.

Fig. 3.59 Three simply-connected regions.

3.6 Cauchy Integral Theorem For Simply Connected Regions

195

Fig. 3.60 Three multiply connected regions. In the figure on the left a path is shown that is
not homotopic to a point. In the remaining two pictures two non-homotopic paths relative
to the region are shown.

Suppose that Î© is a simply connected region, f is analytic on Î© , Î± and Î² are
two points in Î© , and Î³0 and Î³1 are arbitrary paths in Î© joining Î± to Î² . Since Î³0 and
Î³1 are homotopic relative to Î© , Theorem 3.6.5 implies that the integral of f over Î³0
is equal to its integral over Î³1 . In other words, the integral of f is independent of
path in Î© .

analytic function on a simply connected region Î© . If Î³ is a closed path in Î© , then

3.7 Cauchyâ€™s Theorem for Multiply Connected Regions
For subsequent applications, we prove a version of Cauchyâ€™s theorem that involves
several simple paths arranged as follows: C is a simple closed path and C1 ,C2 . . . ,Cn
are disjoint simple closed paths, contained in the interior of C and such that the
interior regions of any two C j â€™s have no common points. To simplify our study, we
require that C and all Cj s have the same positive orientation.
An interesting situation arises when the C j â€™s
contain in their interior parts of the complement of a region Î© and C contains all C j . For
instance Î© could be the shaded region in Figure 3.61. Our goal in this section is to prove
Cauchyâ€™s theorem for such paths. To do so, we
need a theorem from topology which, like the
Jordan curve theorem, although it is very intuitive, it is quite difficult to prove. For a proof
of the next theorem we refer to Selected Topics
in the Classical Theory of Functions of a Complex Variable by Maurice Heins, Dover (2015),
republication of the edition by Holt, Rinehart
& Winston, New York, 1962.

Fig. 3.61 The domain Î© contains
the region interior to C and exterior
to all C j â€™s.

simple non-closed path Î³ . Then U \ Î³ is also a region, i.e., it is open and connected.

setting of multiply connected domains.

path C, are determined by the values of f on the curve C, and the way to recapture
the values inside C is to integrate f (Î¶ ) against the function 1/[2Ï€ i(Î¶ âˆ’ z)] on C.
Something analogous is valid for the derivatives of f . To achieve this we need to
know how to differentiate under the integral sign.

We end the section with some important theoretical applications of Cauchyâ€™s formula. The first result is already contained in Theorem 3.8.6 but deserves a separate
statement because of its importance.
Corollary 3.8.9. Suppose that f is analytic in an open set Î© . Then f has derivatives
of all orders f  , f  , f (3) , f (4) , . . . which are analytic functions in Î© .
Proof. For z in Î© , let BR (z) be a closed disk contained in Î© . Pick C to be the
boundary of this closed disk. By Theorem 3.8.6, all derivatives of f exist in Î© \C,
and in particular at the given point z. These derivatives are shown in (3.8.10).

5

2

Consider the function h(t) = t 3 , âˆ’âˆž < t < âˆž. Its derivative, f  (t) = 53 t 3 , exists
and is continuous for all real t; however, f  (t) does not exist at t = 0. Thus Corollary 3.8.9 has no analog in the theory of functions of a real variable.
The following is a converse to Cauchyâ€™s theorem. It is named after the Italian
mathematician Giacinto Morera (1856â€“1907).
Theorem 3.8.10. (Moreraâ€™s Theorem) Let f be a continuous complex-valued function on a region Î© . Suppose that for all closed disks B contained in Î© and for all
closed paths Î³ in B we have
Î³

f (z) dz = 0 .

(3.8.11)

Then the function f is analytic on Î© .
Proof. For every z âˆˆ Î© there is a disk Br (z) contained in Î© . Since (3.8.11) holds for
all closed paths Î³ contained in Br (z), applying Theorem 3.3.4, we obtain that there
is an analytic function F on Br (z) such that F  = f . By Corollary 3.8.9, f is analytic

in Br (z). Since z is arbitrary in Î© , it follows that f is analytic in Î© .

3.9 Bounds for Moduli of Analytic Functions
In this section, we present several fundamental results about analytic functions,
including the famous Liouville theorem, and the maximum modulus principle. We
also give a simple proof of the fundamental theorem of algebra.

The following surprising application of Cauchyâ€™s estimate was proved by the
French Mathematician Joseph Liouville (1809â€“1882). Recall that a function is
called entire if it is analytic on all of C.
Theorem 3.9.2. (Liouvilleâ€™s Theorem) A bounded entire function must be constant.
Proof. Let f be an entire function satisfying | f (z)| â‰¤ M for all z âˆˆ C. Applying
Cauchyâ€™s estimate to f  on a disk of radius R > 0 around a point z0 âˆˆ C, we obtain

| f  (z0 )| â‰¤ M
R . Letting R â†’ âˆž, we obtain that f (z0 ) = 0. Since z0 is arbitrary, it
follows that f  (z) = 0 for all z, and hence, f is constant as a consequence of Theorem 2.5.7.

Here is one useful application of Liouvilleâ€™s theorem.
Corollary 3.9.3. If f is entire and | f (z)| â†’ âˆž as |z| â†’ âˆž, then f must have at least
one zero.
Proof. Suppose to the contrary that f has no zeros in C. Then g = 1/ f is also entire
and |g(z)| â†’ 0 as |z| â†’ âˆž. This property implies that g is bounded on C (Exercise
14). By Theorem 3.9.2, g is constant and consequently f is constant.

The exponential function ez is entire and never equals to 0. As a consequence of
Corollary 3.9.3, we deduce that |ez | does not tend to infinity as |z| â†’ âˆž, although
e|z| tends to infinity as |z| â†’ âˆž.

We now turn our attention to the fundamental theorem of algebra, first proved by
Gauss in 1799 (with a topological gap) and then again by him in 1816 in two other
ways different from the first one.
Theorem 3.9.4. (Fundamental Theorem of Algebra) Every polynomial of degree
n â‰¥ 1 has exactly n zeros counted according to multiplicity.
Proof. It is enough to show that every polynomial p of degree n â‰¥ 1 has at least one
zero. For, if we know that z0 is a zero of p, then we write p(z) = (z âˆ’ z0 )q(z), where
q is a polynomial of degree n âˆ’ 1 (Exercise 13). We continue factoring until we have
written p as the product of n linear terms times a constant, which shows that p has
exactly n roots. So let us show that p has at least one zero. If an = 0, write

hence |p(z)| â†’ âˆž. We complete the proof by applying Corollary 3.9.3.

Example 3.9.7 shows that the modulus of an analytic function need not attain its
maximum on the boundary, and the maximum value of the modulus on the boundary
may not be the maximum value inside the region. As we now show, the situation is
different if Î© is bounded. In this case, the answers to both questions above are
affirmative.
Corollary 3.9.8. (Maximum Modulus Principle) Suppose that Î© is a bounded
region and f is analytic on Î© and continuous on its closure Î© . Then
(i) | f | attains its maximum M on the boundary of Î© , and
(ii) either f is constant or | f (z)| < M for all z in Î© .
Proof. If f is constant then (i) and (ii) hold obviously. Suppose f is not constant.
The set consisting of Î© and its boundary is closed and bounded. Since | f | is continuous on this set, it attains its maximum M on this set. But Theorem 3.9.6 says | f |
cannot attain its maximum in Î© , so | f | attains its maximum on the boundary of Î©
and | f | < M in Î© .

The modulus of a nonconstant analytic function can attain its minimum on a
region Î© . Consider, for example, the function z on the open disk |z| < 1. Then
the minimum of |z| is 0 and it is attained at z = 0. However, if the function never
vanishes in Î© , then we have the following useful principle.
Theorem 3.9.9. Suppose that f is nonvanishing and analytic on a region Î© . If | f |
attains a minimum in Î© , then f is constant in Î© .
Proof. Apply the maximum modulus principle to g = 1/ f .



Combining the previous two results, we obtain the following principle.
Corollary 3.9.10. Suppose that Î© is a bounded region and f is analytic and nonvanishing on Î© and continuous on Î© . Then
(i) | f | attains a maximum M and minimum m on the boundary of Î© , and
(ii) either f is constant or m < | f (z)| < M for all z in Î© .

Chapter 4

Series of Analytic Functions and Singularities
If, for every increasing value of n, the sum Sn indefinitely
approaches a certain limit S, the series will be called convergent, and the limit in question will be called the sum of
the series. If, on the contrary, while n increases indefinitely,
the sum Sn does not approach a fixed limit, the series will be
divergent and will no longer have a sum.
-Augustin-Louis Cauchy (1789â€“1857)
(Cauchyâ€™s definition of a convergent series from his Cours dâ€™
analyse.
In calculus, we use Taylor series to represent functions on intervals centered at
fixed points with a radius of convergence that could be positive, infinite, or zero,
depending on the remainder associated with the function. For example, cos x, ex ,
2
1
, and the function defined by eâˆ’1/x for x = 0 and 0 if x = 0 are all infinitely
1+x2
differentiable for all real x. The radius of convergence of the Taylor series representation around zero is âˆž for the first two, 1 for the third one, and 0 for the last one.
However in complex analysis, Taylor series are much nicer, in the sense that the
remainder will play no role in determining their convergence. If a function is analytic on a disk of radius R centered at z0 , then it has a Taylor series representation
1
centered at z0 with radius at least R. For example the function 1+z
2 is analytic on
the disk |z| < 1 but, as it is not differentiable at z = Â±i, we do not expect the series
to have a radius of convergence larger than 1.
Taylor series, and more generally Laurent series, are used to study important
properties of analytic functions, concerning their zeros and isolated problem points
(singularities). They are also useful in studying properties of special functions, such
as Bessel functions.
The theory of power series as described in this chapter owes a lot to the German
mathematician Karl Weierstrass (1815â€“1897). Weierstrass introduced to analysis
the (Îµ , Î´ )-notation in proofs, replacing Cauchyâ€™s terminology, such as â€œindefinitely
approaches a certain limitâ€ and â€œincreases indefinitely.â€ Weierstrassâ€™ contributions
to analysis are evidenced by the number of fundamental results we encounter here
that bear his name.

4.1 Sequences and Series of Functions
In Section 1.5 we considered sequences and series of complex numbers. Now we
turn our attention to sequences and series of functions.
Suppose that fn and f are complex-valued functions defined on a subset E âŠ‚ C.
We say that fn converges pointwise to f on E, if limnâ†’âˆž fn (z)= f (z) for every z in E.
Â© Springer International Publishing AG, part of Springer Nature 2018

Hence fn converges pointwise to f on E if for every z in E and every Îµ > 0 we can
find N > 0 (depending on z and Îµ ) such that for all n â‰¥ N, we have | f (z)âˆ’ fn (z)| < Îµ .
It is important to notice that the integer N depends in general on both z and Îµ .
Suppose un is defined on a set E âŠ‚ C. The series of functions âˆ‘âˆž
n=1 un is said to
converge pointwise if the sequence of partial sums sn = âˆ‘nk=1 uk converges pointwise on E.
In general, pointwise convergence does not preserve some desirable properties
of the functions fn . For example, the pointwise limit of continuous functions may
not be continuous; and the pointwise limit of analytic functions may not be analytic. These properties are preserved, however, by the following stronger mode of
convergence.
Definition 4.1.1. Let { fn }âˆž
n=1 be a sequence of functions. We say that f n converges
uniformly to f on E, and we write limnâ†’âˆž fn = f uniformly on E, if for every Îµ > 0
we can find N > 0 such that for all n â‰¥ N and all z in E, we have | fn (z) âˆ’ f (z)| < Îµ .
A series of functions âˆ‘âˆž
n=1 un is said to converge uniformly on E if the sequence
of partial sums sn = âˆ‘nk=1 uk converges uniformly on E.
The key words in Definition 4.1.1 are â€œfor all z in E.â€ These require that fn (z)
be close to f (z) for all z in E simultaneously. Let Mn = max | fn (z) âˆ’ f (z)|, where
the maximum is taken over all z in E. If no maximum is attained, we set1 Mn =
sup{| fn (z) âˆ’ f (z)| : z âˆˆ E}. An equivalent formulation of uniform convergence is

There is an important point to be made about part (c) of Example 4.1.2. Clearly
we could have replaced the left endpoint x = 0.1 by any number a with 0 < a < 1
and still had uniform convergence on [a, 1]. While the sequence failed to converge
uniformly on [0, 1], it does converge uniformly on any proper closed subinterval
[a, 1] where 0 < a < 1. This is a common phenomenon that one encounters with
many sequences or series. They may fail to converge uniformly on a whole region,
but they may converge uniformly on all closed (and bounded) proper subregions.

230

4 Series of Analytic Functions and Singularities

We now examine the preservation of continuity and integrability in the context
of uniform limits of functions.
Theorem 4.1.3. (i) Suppose that fn â†’ f uniformly on E and fn is continuous on E
for every n. Then f is continuous on E.
(ii) Suppose u = âˆ‘âˆž

Sequences and Series of Analytic Functions
The remaining results of this section concern limits of analytic functions. Based
on the behavior of the geometric series (Example 4.1.9), we study the limit under
the assumption of uniform convergence on closed subdisks. This hypothesis is less
restrictive than uniform convergence. The following is a central result in the theory
of analytic functions. Its proof is a nice application of Moreraâ€™s theorem (Theorem 3.8.10).
Theorem 4.1.10. Suppose that { fn }âˆž
n=1 is a sequence of analytic functions on a
region Î© such that fn â†’ f uniformly on every closed disk contained in Î© . Then
(i) f is analytic on Î© , and
(k)

derivative is the kth derivative of the limit.

Theorem 4.1.10 may fail if we replace analytic functions by differentiable functions of a real variable. That is, if E is a subset of the real line and fn (x) â†’ f (x)
uniformly on E, it does not follow in general that fn converges to f , as the next
example shows.
Example 4.1.12. (Failure of termwise differentiation) For 0 â‰¤ x â‰¤ 2Ï€ and n =
inx
1, 2, . . . define fn (x) = ein . It is clear that fn â†’ 0 uniformly on [0, 2Ï€ ]. But
fn (x) = einx and this sequence does not converge except at x = 0 or x = 2Ï€ . (See
Example 1.5.9) Consequently, fn does not converge to 0. Can we understand how
this occurs within the larger framework of complex functions? Replace x by z and
inz
consider the sequence functions fn (z) = ein . We cannot find a complex neighborhood of the real interval [0, 2Ï€ ] where fn converges, as such a neighborhood would
contain z with Im z < 0. Thus Theorem 4.1.10 does not apply.

(a) Let Î´ > 1 be a positive real number. Show that the series converges uniformly on every halfplane HÎ´ = {z : Re z â‰¥ Î´ > 1}.

Riemann used the zeta function to study the distribution of the prime numbers. Although this
function was previously known to Euler, Riemann was the first one to consider it over the complex
numbers. An important thesis, conjectured by Riemann, is the Riemann hypothesis, which states
that the analytic continuation of the zeta function has infinitely many nonreal roots that lie on the
line Re z = 12 .

Definition 4.2.1. A power series is a series of the form
âˆž

We adhere to the usual notational convention that (z âˆ’ z0 )0 = 1 for all z. A power
series always converges at the center z = z0 because all the terms vanish for n â‰¥ 1.
The term with n = 0 may not vanish but produces the constant c0 . For z = z0 the
power series may converge or diverge. Let us consider a few examples.

Remark 4.2.7. The radius of convergence of a power series âˆ‘âˆž
n=0 cn (z âˆ’ z0 ) is

at z, which is impossible.

4.3 Taylor Series
In the previous section, we showed that a power series is analytic on its disk of
convergence. In this section, we prove essentially the converse that every analytic
function can be expressed as a power series when restricted to a disk contained in
its domain of analyticity. We are going to achieve this goal by reducing matters to
the expansion of the geometric series

Remark 4.3.2. The coefficient f n!(z0 ) is called the nth Taylor coefficient of f . Taylor series are named after the English mathematician Brook Taylor (1685â€“1731),
who wrote about them in 1715. The idea of expanding a function by a power series
was known to mathematicians before Taylor and appeared in the work of Sir Isaac
Newton (1643â€“1727), the Scottish mathematician James Gregory (1638â€“1675),
and the Swiss mathematician Johann Bernoulli (1667â€“1748). Maclaurin series are
named after the Scottish mathematician Colin Maclaurin (1698â€“1746), who used
them and acknowledged that they are special cases of Taylor series. When z0 = 0 in
Theorem 4.3.1, the series is called the Maclaurin series of f .

Example 4.3.4 shows the advantage of complex Taylor series over their real counterparts. A real Taylor series converges if and only if a certain remainder goes to
zero. In the complex case, the remainder is irrelevant; the Taylor series will converge in the largest disk that you can fit inside the domain of definition of the analytic function.

will allow us to turn well-known Taylor series from calculus into Taylor series of
complex-valued functions.

Factoring Zeros and Extending Analytic Functions
When a polynomial p has a zero at z0 , we can write p(z) = (z âˆ’ z0 )g(z), where g is
another polynomial. In particular, the function p(z)/(z âˆ’ z0 ) (which equals g(z) for
all z = z0 ) can be made analytic even at z0 by defining it to be g(z0 ) at z0 . The same
is true for analytic functions in general.

38. Project Problem: The Catalan numbers. The Catalan numbers are named after the French
mathematician EugeÌ€ne Charles Catalan (1814â€“1894). They arise in many combinatorics problems,
such as the following:
â€¢ The number of ways in which parentheses can be placed in a sequence of numbers to be multiplied, two at a time.
â€¢ The number of ways a polygon with n+1 sides can be cut into nâˆ’1 triangles by nonintersecting
diagonals.
â€¢ The number of paths of length 2(n âˆ’ 1) through an n by n grid that connect two diagonally
opposite vertices that stay below or on the main diagonal.
Denote the nth Catalan number by cn . Take the first interpretation of these numbers, and say we
have a sequence of three numbers a, b, c. We can multiply them two at a time in exactly two
ways: ((ab)c) and (a(bc)). Thus c3 = 2. With a sequence of four numbers, we can have (((ab)c)d)
or ((ab)(cd)) or ((a(bc))d) or (a((bc)d)) or (a(b(cd))). Thus c4 = 5. In general, if we have a
sequence of n numbers, we can break it into two subsequences of k and n âˆ’ k numbers. Then
each arrangement of parentheses from the k sequence can be combined with an arrangement of
parentheses of the n âˆ’ k sequence to yield an arrangement of parentheses of the n-sequence. Since

260

4 Series of Analytic Functions and Singularities

we have ck possibilities for the k-sequence and cnâˆ’k possibilities for the (n âˆ’ k)-sequence, we get
this way ck Ã— cnâˆ’k possibilities for the n-sequence. But this can be done for k = 1, 2, . . . , n âˆ’ 1, and
so we have the recursion relation

41. Analytic continuation. We briefly visit discuss the topic of analytic continuation. By constructing a Taylor series of a function f about z0 , we create a function g analytic on some disk
centered at z0 , such that g and f coincide near z0 . If g is defined in a region where f is not, or if g
has different values than f away from z0 , g is said to be an analytic continuation of f .
As a somewhat contrived example, suppose that ez is defined only for |z| < 1. Can we find a
function that coincides with ez on the unit disk but is analytic elsewhere? It is clear that we can (just
define the exponential function on C), but let us see how we can get this from series expansions.
zn
z
The Maclaurin series of ez is g(z) = âˆ‘âˆž
n=0 n! , which converges for all z. Thus g(z) = e for all z is
an analytic continuation of f (z). We now present a more interesting case of analytic continuation
of logarithm branches.

To see this, consider the branch f1 (z) = logâˆ’ Ï€ z. Since f = f1 in a neighborhood of z0 , their
4
âˆš
Taylor expansions about z0 will be the same, and since f1 is analytic on the disk |z + 1 âˆ’ i| < 2,
Theorem 4.3.1 says that g must equal f1 in this disk. Thus, for z in the part of the disk above
the branch cut (quadrants 1 and 2) we have g(z) = f1 (z) = f (z), but for z in the part of the disk
below the branch cut (quadrant 3) we have g(z) = f1 (z) = f (z) + 2Ï€ i. The Taylor expansion of f
analytically continues the function into quadrant 3 where arg z > Ï€ , and the analytic continuation
is different from the original function.

4.4 Laurent Series
We saw in the previous section that if a function is analytic in a disk centered at
a point z0 , then it has a Taylor series representation in that disk. There is a similar series representation in terms of both positive and negative powers of (z âˆ’ z0 )
for functions that are analytic in annular regions around a point z0 . These series
are known as Laurent series, after the French engineer and mathematician Pierre
Alphonse Laurent (1813â€“1854), who discovered them around 1842.
For 0 â‰¤ R1 < R2 â‰¤ âˆž we define the annular region (or annulus)
AR R z

See Figure 4.10 for an illustration where R1
and R2 are nonzero and finite. Note that the
annulus AR1 ,R2 (z0 ) degenerates into a punctured disk with z0 removed when R1 = 0 and
R2 < âˆž, a punctured plane with z0 removed
when R1 = 0, R2 = âˆž, or a plane with a disk
centered at z0 cut out of it when 0 < R1 and
R2 = âˆž. These sets still count as annuli by our
definition.

In the previous examples, we were given the function and the annular region on
which it is analytic. If the region is not known and we are asked to find a Laurent
series expansion about a point, our first step would be to determine the annular
regions on which the function is analytic. As the next example illustrates, to each
region there corresponds a different Laurent series.

series, an (z âˆ’ z0 )n , is analytic on the annulus AR1 ,R2 (z0 ), and the series converges

term the series as many times as we want in the annulus AR1 ,R2 (z0 ). So, for example,

4.5 Zeros and Singularities
In this section, we use Taylor and Laurent series to study the zeros and singular
points of analytic functions. To motivate the first result, suppose that p is a polynomial and p(z0 ) = 0, then we can factor the highest power of (z âˆ’ z0 ) out of p(z)
and write p(z) = q(z)(z âˆ’ z0 )m , where q is a polynomial with q(z0 ) = 0. It is also
straightforward to check that p( j) (z0 ) = 0 for all j = 0, 1, . . . , m âˆ’ 1.
Definition 4.5.1. Let Î© be a region, let f be an analytic function defined on Î© , and
let z0 âˆˆ Î© . Let m â‰¥ 1 be an integer. The complex number z0 is called a zero of order
m of f if there is another analytic function g defined in a neighborhood of z0 such
that g(z0 ) = 0 and

4.5 Zeros and Singularities

273

f (z) = (z âˆ’ z0 )m g(z)
for all z in this neighborhood. If m = 1, we call z0 a simple zero of f . The zero z0 is
called isolated if there is a neighborhood of z0 in Î© such that z0 is the only zero of
f in this neighborhood.
Theorem 4.5.2. Suppose that f is analytic on a region Î© that contains a point z0
and f (z0 ) = 0. Then exactly one of the following two assertions holds:
(i) f is identically zero in a neighborhood of z0 .
(ii) z0 is an isolated zero of f .
Moreover, if (ii) holds, then there are an integer m â‰¥ 1, a real number r > 0, and
an analytic function Î» on Br (z0 ) such that Î» (z) = 0 for all z âˆˆ Br (z0 ) and
f (z) = (z âˆ’ z0 )m Î» (z)

for all z âˆˆ Br (z0 ).

We now address the following question: Suppose that a function is analytic and not
identically zero on a region. Can it have zeros that are not isolated? The answer is
no, and this depends on the fact that the region is connected.
Theorem 4.5.4. An nonzero analytic function defined on a region has isolated zeros.
Proof. Let f be an analytic function defined on a region Î© . Define the subsets

Î©0 = {z âˆˆ Î© : f (z) = 0 and z is not isolated}
Î©1 = {z âˆˆ Î© : f (z) = 0} âˆª {z âˆˆ Î© : f (z) = 0 and z is isolated}.
Then Î©0 and Î©1 are disjoint sets whose union is Î© . We show that Î©0 and Î©1 are
open; then by the connectedness of Î© (Proposition 2.1.7) we have either Î© = Î©0
or Î© = Î©1 . If Î© = Î©0 , then every point in Î© is a zero of f ; in this case f vanishes
everywhere on Î© . If Î© = Î©1 then every point of Î© is either not a zero or an isolated
zero of f . In this case all zeros of f are isolated.
If w âˆˆ Î©0 , then assertion (ii) in Theorem 4.5.2 does not hold, hence assertion
(i) must hold and thus f vanishes identically in some neighborhood Br (w). Clearly,
all the points in Br (w) are not isolated zeros of f , so Br (w) is contained in Î©0 .
This shows that Î©0 is open in this case. Now let w âˆˆ Î©1 . If f (w) = 0, then there
is a neighborhood of w on which f is nonvanishing; this neighborhood is contained
in Î©1 . If f (w) = 0 and w is an isolated zero, then Theorem 4.5.2 guarantees the
existence of a neighborhood BÎ´ (w) of w on which f has no zeros except the isolated
one at w. This neighborhood is also contained in Î©1 , since BÎ´ (w) = BÎ´ (w) \ {w} is

contained in {z âˆˆ Î© : f (z) = 0}. Thus Î©1 is open.
Theorem 4.5.5. (Identity Principle) Suppose that f , g are analytic functions on a
region Î© . If {zn }âˆž
n=1 is an sequence of distinct points in Î© with f (zn ) = g(zn ) for
all n and zn â†’ z0 âˆˆ Î© , then f (z) = g(z) for all z âˆˆ Î© .
Proof. If zn is a zero of f âˆ’ g in Î© and zn â†’ z0 as n â†’ âˆž, then f (zn ) = g(zn ) for all
n and by continuity it follows that

4.5 Zeros and Singularities

f (z0 ) = f ( lim zn ) = lim f (zn ) = lim g(zn ) = g( lim zn ) = g(z0 )

In many interesting applications of Theorem 4.5.5 the functions f and g are equal
on an interval [a, b] of the real line, or on a whole disk BR (z0 ). Such sets clearly
contain infinite sequences of points that converge to a point in the set itself.
Example 4.5.6. (Applications of the identity principle) (a) Recall the identity
cos2 z + sin2 z = 1 valid for all complex numbers z; see (1.7.9) in Proposition 1.7.3.
We prove this identity using Theorem 4.5.5 and the fact that it holds for real
numbers z. Let f (z) = cos2 z + sin2 z and g(z) = 1. Clearly, f and g are entire, and
for real z = x, we have f (x) = sin2 x + cos2 x = 1 = g(x). Since f (z) = g(z) for all z
on the real line, which contains infinite converging sequences, we infer from Theorem 4.5.5 that f (z) = g(z) for all z; that is, cos2 z + sin2 z = 1.
(b) Modifying the method in (a), we can prove identities involving two or more
variables. As an illustration, let us prove that for all complex numbers z1 and z2 ,
cos(z1 + z2 ) = cos z1 cos z2 âˆ’ sin z1 sin z2 .

(4.5.1)

In a first step, let z2 = x2 be an arbitrary real number. Let f (z) = cos(z + x2 ) and
g(z) = cos z cos x2 âˆ’ sin z sin x2 . Clearly, f and g are entire, and from the addition
formula for the cosines, we have f (x) = g(x) for all real x. Hence by Theorem 4.5.5,
we have f (z) = g(z) for all z; equivalently,
cos(z + x2 ) = cos z cos x2 âˆ’ sin z sin x2

for all complex z.

(4.5.2)

Now fix z1 and define F(z2 ) = cos(z1 + z2 ) and G(z2 ) = cos z1 cos z2 âˆ’ sin z1 sin z2 .
Here again, F and G are entire (as functions of z2 ) and (4.5.2) states that F and G
agree on the real line. By Theorem 4.5.5, F(z2 ) = G(z2 ) for all complex z2 , implying
that (4.5.1) holds.

To establish other useful consequences regarding the number of zeros of an analytic functions, we need a topological property of complex numbers known as the
Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem. This states the following: Let S denote a closed and
bounded subset of C, and let {wn }âˆž

Corollary 4.5.7. An analytic function defined on a bounded region that admits a
continuous extension on its closure and is nonvanishing on the boundary of the
region has at most finitely many zeros.

276

4 Series of Analytic Functions and Singularities

Proof. The closure Î© of a region Î© is the region Î© together with its boundary.
If Î© is bounded, then Î© is closed and bounded. Suppose that f is a function as
in the statement of the theorem and suppose that it has infinitely many zeros in
Î© ; then by the Bolzano-Weierstrass theorem, there is an infinite sequence of zeros,
{zn }âˆž
n=1 in Î© that converges to a point z0 in Î© . Since f is continuous in Î© , f (z0 ) =
limnâ†’âˆž f (zn ) = 0, and since f is nonvanishing on the boundary, we conclude that
z0 lies in Î© . Hence by Theorem 4.5.5, f is identically zero on Î© , and since f is
continuous, f must be zero on the boundary, which is a contradiction. Hence f can

have at most finitely many zeros in Î© .

Isolated Singularities
If a function is analytic in a neighborhood of a point z0 except possibly at the pointz0 ,
then z0 is called an isolated singularity of the function. There are only three different types of isolated singularities, which are defined as follows.
Definition 4.5.8. Suppose that z0 is an isolated singularity of an analytic function f
defined in a deleted neighborhood of z0 . Then
(i) z0 is a removable singularity of f if the function can be redefined at z0 to be
analytic there.
(ii) z0 is a pole of f if lim | f (z)| = âˆž.
zâ†’z0

(iii) z0 is an essential singularity of f if it is neither a pole nor a removable singularity.

When redefining f at a removable singularity z0 , we set f (z0 ) = limzâ†’z0 f (z);
otherwise, f would not be continuous and hence could not be analytic at z0 . Note
that when z0 is a removable singularity, f must be bounded near z0 (Figure 4.16).
The singularity is a pole if the graph of | f | blows up to infinity as we approach z0
(Figure 4.17). It is harder to explain the graph of an essential singularity, but as the

One way to determine whether an isolated singularity is an essential singularity is
to rule out the possibility of the other two types of singularities. This can be achieved
by showing that the function is unbounded and has different limits as we approach
the isolated singularity in different ways. The following theorem is a useful characterization of essential singularities. The theorem was discovered independently by
Weierstrass and the Italian mathematician Felice Casorati (1835â€“1890).
Theorem 4.5.19. (Casorati-Weierstrass) Suppose that f is analytic on a punctured disk BR (z0 ). Then z0 is an essential singularity of f if and only if the following
two conditions hold:
(i) There is a sequence {zn }âˆž
n=1 in BR (z0 ) such that zn â†’ z0 and | f (zn )| â†’ âˆž as
n â†’ âˆž.
(ii) For any complex number Î± , there is a sequence {zn }âˆž
n=1 in BR (z0 ) (that depends
on Î± ) such that zn â†’ z0 and f (zn ) â†’ Î± as n â†’ âˆž.

4.5 Zeros and Singularities

283

Remark 4.5.20. Notice that (i) is not saying that limzâ†’z0 | f (z)| = âˆž. It is just saying
that you can approach z0 in such a way that | f (z)| will tend to infinity. Similarly, part
(ii) says that you can approach z0 in such a way that f (z) comes arbitrarily close to
any complex value Î± . In fact, a deep result in complex analysis, known as Picardâ€™s
great theorem, states that, in a neighborhood of an essential singularity, a function
takes on every complex value, with one possible exception, an infinite number of
times.
Proof. If (i) holds, then f is not bounded near z0 , and so z0 is not a removable
singularity. If (ii) holds, then it is not the case that limzâ†’z0 | f (z)| = âˆž, and so z0 is
not a pole. Thus (i) and (ii) together imply that z0 is an essential singularity.
Conversely, let z0 be an essential singularity. Since f is not bounded near z0
(otherwise z0 would be a removable singularity), it follows that (i) holds. Now we
prove (ii). Precisely, we show that

We end the section by extending the definitions of zeros and singularities to the
point at infinity. If f is analytic on a neighborhood of infinity, i.e., f is analytic for
all |z| > R, then f 1z is analytic in the annulus 0 < |z| < R1 and hence it has an
isolated singularity at 0. With this in mind, we make the following definitions.
Definition 4.5.22. Suppose that f is analytic for all |z| > R. Then f has
 
a removable singularity at âˆž if f 1z has a removable singularity at 0;
 
a pole of order m at âˆž if f 1z has a pole of order m at 0;
 
an essential singularity at âˆž if f 1z has a essential singularity at 0.

4.6 Schwarzâ€™s Lemma
In this section we put together some of the material we have developed to prove
a very elegant and useful lemma attributed to Karl Hermann Amandus Schwarz
(1843â€“1921). This lemma reflects the rigidity of analytic functions from the unit
disk to itself and has remarkable applications.
Lemma 4.6.1. (Schwarzâ€™s Lemma) Suppose that f is analytic on the open unit

Chapter 5

Residue Theory
After having thought about this subject, and brought together
the diverse results mentioned above, I had the hope of establishing on a direct and rigorous analysis the passage from the real
to the imaginary; and my research has lead me to this Memoire.
-Augustin-Louis Cauchy (1789â€“1857)
[Writing about his Memoire of 1814, which contained the
residue theorem and several computations of real integrals by
complex methods.]
In previous chapters, we introduced complex functions and studied properties of
three essential tools: derivatives, integrals, and series of complex functions. In this
chapter, we derive some exciting applications of complex analysis based on one formula, known as Cauchyâ€™s residue theorem. We have indirectly used this result when
computing integrals in previous sections. Here we highlight the main ideas behind
it and devise new techniques for computing Laplace transforms and integrals that
 âˆž sin x
âˆž
âˆ’x2 dx
arise in Fourier series. For instance, integrals like âˆ’âˆž
x dx and 0 (cos x)e
are often tedious to compute via real-variable techniques. With the residue theorem
and some additional estimates with complex functions, the computations of these
integrals become straightforward tasks.
In Section 5.7, we use residues to expand our knowledge of analytic functions.
We use integrals to count the number of zeros of analytic functions and give a formula for the inverse of analytic functions. Theoretical results, such as the open mapping property, are derived and used to obtain a fresh and different perspective on
concrete results such as the maximum modulus principle.
The residue theorem was discovered by Cauchy around 1814 but was explicitly
stated in 1831. Cauchyâ€™s goal was to place under one umbrella the computations of
certain special integrals, some of which involving complex substitutions, that were
calculated by Euler, Laplace, Legendre, and others.

So far the examples that we treated involved residues at poles of finite order.
There is no formula like (5.1.6) for computing the residue at an essential singularity.
We have to rely on various tricks to evaluate the coefficient aâˆ’1 in the Laurent series
expansion. We illustrate with several examples, starting with a useful observation.
Proposition 5.1.9. Suppose that 0 is an isolated singularity of an even function f
that is analytic on C \ {0}. Then Res ( f , 0) = 0.

The success of the method of contour integration crucially depends on the choice of
contours. In the previous examples, we used expanding semicircular contours. To
evaluate integrals involving exponential functions, we use rectangular contours. We
consider integrals of the form

by eax /c, hence it is integrable near âˆ’âˆž as well.

In Example 5.5.3, the point of introducing different branches of the multiple1

a point on the branch cut depending on how we approach this point. Consider, for

The next result bears the name the counting theorem because it counts the number of zeros (according to multiplicity) of an analytic function inside a simple path.
Counting zeros according to multiplicity means that each zero is counted as many
times as its order.

Example 5.7.5. (The argument principle) Find the number of zeros of the polynomial z6 + 6z + 10 in the first quadrant.

Since the given polynomial has finitely many zeros (at most six), if R is large

so that no roots lie on the circular arc Î³R . We need to prove that there are no roots on

As an application of the counting principle we derive a famous result known
as RoucheÌâ€™s theorem, named after the French mathematician and educator EugeÌ€ne
RoucheÌ (1832â€“1910). We need the following lemma.
Lemma 5.7.8. Let Ï† be a continuous function on a region Î© that takes only integer
values. Then Ï† is constant in Î© .
Proof. Assume that Ï† is not constant, and let z1 and z2 in Î© be such that Ï† (z1 ) =
n1 < Ï† (z2 ) = n2 . Let r be a real number such that n1 < r < n2 . Since Ï† is continuous,
the sets
A = {z âˆˆ Î© : Ï† (z) < r}
B = {z âˆˆ Î© : Ï† (z) > r}
are open. Also, z1 âˆˆ A and z2 âˆˆ B, hence A and B are nonemtpy. They are also
disjoint and satisfy A âˆª B = Î© . This contradicts the fact that Î© is connected. Thus
Ï† is constant.

In what follows, we need a version of Lemma 5.7.8, where Ï† is a continuous,
integer-valued function on an interval [a, b]. The preceding proof can be easily modified to cover this case.
Theorem 5.7.9. (RoucheÌâ€™s Theorem) Suppose that C is a simple closed path, Î© is
the region inside C, f and g are analytic inside and on C. If |g(z)| < | f (z)| for all z
on C, then
N( f + g) = N( f )
in other words, f + g and f have the same number of zeros on Î© .

5.7 The Counting Theorem and RoucheÌâ€™s Theorem

357

Proof. For z on C, the inequality |g(z)| < | f (z)| implies that f (z) = 0, and so |g|/| f |
is continuous and strictly less than 1 on C. Since C is a closed and bounded set,
|g|/| f | attains its maximum Î´ < 1 on C. Pick Î´ < Î´1 < 1. For z on C and 0 < |Î» | â‰¤
1/Î´1 , if f (z) + Î» g(z) = 0, then |g(z)|/| f (z)| = 1/|Î» | â‰¥ Î´1 , which contradicts that
|g|/| f | â‰¤ Î´ on C. So f (z) + Î» g(z) is not equal to zero for all z on C and |Î» | â‰¤ 1/Î´1 .

in z is close to that of G(Î»  , z) in z. By Theorem 5.7.2, Ï† (Î» ) = N( f + Î» g), and so

The following are typical applications of RoucheÌâ€™s theorem.
Example 5.7.10. (Counting zeros with RoucheÌâ€™s theorem)
(a) Show that all zeros of p(z) = z4 + 6z + 3 lie inside the circle |z| = 2.
(b) Show that if a is a real number with a > e, then in |z| < 1 the equation ez = azn
has n roots (counting orders).
Solution. (a) Since p is a polynomial of degree 4, it is enough to show that N(p) = 4
inside the circle |z| = 2. Take f (z) = z4 and g(z) = 6z + 3 and note that f (z) + g(z) =
p(z). For |z| = 2, we have | f (z)| = 24 = 32 and |g(z)| â‰¤ |6z|+3 = 15. Hence |g(z)| <
| f (z)| for all z on the circle |z| = 2, and so by RoucheÌâ€™s theorem N( f ) = N( f + g) =
N(p). Clearly f has one zero with multiplicity 4 at z = 0. Thus N( f ) = 4 and so
N(p) = 4, as desired.

358

5 Residue Theory

(b) Take f (z) = azn and g(z) = âˆ’ez . Counted according to multiplicity, f has n zeros
in |z| < 1 and so N( f ) = n. For |z| = 1, | f (z)| = |azn | = a and


|g(z)| = |ez | = ecos Î¸ +i sin Î¸  = ecos Î¸ â‰¤ e < a.
Thus |g(z)| < | f (z)| for all |z| = 1 and so by RoucheÌâ€™s theorem n = N(azn ) =

N(azn âˆ’ ez ), which implies that azn âˆ’ ez = 0 has n roots in |z| < 1.

of algebra.

N( f ), the number of zeros of f in the region |z| < R, is the same as N( f + g), the
number of zeros of f + g. But N( f ) = n and f + g = p, so N(p) = n showing that p
has exactly n zeros.

The Local Mapping Theorem
In this part, we investigate fundamental properties of the inverse function of analytic functions When do they exist? Are they analytic? Are there explicit formulas
for them? All these questions can be answered with the help of RoucheÌâ€™s theorem
and the counting theorem. Our investigation leads to interesting new properties of
analytic functions and shed new light on some classical results studied earlier. In
particular, we give a simple proof of the maximum modulus principle. Interesting
applications of these topics are presented in the exercises, including a formula due

5.7 The Counting Theorem and RoucheÌâ€™s Theorem

359

to Lagrange for the inverse of analytic functions with some of its applications to the
solution to transcendental equations.
Suppose that f is analytic at z0 and let w0 = f (z0 ). We say that f has order m â‰¥ 1
at z0 if the zero of f (z) âˆ’ w0 has order m at z0 . The order of f at z0 is the order of the
first nonvanishing term in the Taylor series expansion, past the constant term. Thus
f has order m at z0 if and only if f ( j) (z0 ) = 0 for j = 1, . . . , m âˆ’ 1 and f (m) (z0 ) = 0.
In particular, f has order 1 at z0 if and only if f  (z0 ) = 0.
Theorem 5.7.12. (Local Mapping Theorem) Let f be a nonconstant analytic function in a neighborhood of z0 . Let w0 = f (z0 ) and n be the order of f at z0 . Then there
exist R > 0 and Ï > 0 such that for every w âˆˆ BÏ (w0 ) \ {w0 } there are precisely n
distinct points in BR (z0 ) whose image under f is w.
Proof. Since f âˆ’ w0 has a zero of order n â‰¥ 1, write

The case n = 1 of the theorem deserves a separate statement.
Theorem 5.7.13. (Inverse Function Theorem) Suppose that f is analytic on a
region Î© and z0 is in Î© . Then f is one-to-one on some neighborhood Br (z0 ) if
and only if f  (z0 ) = 0.
Proof. If f  (z0 ) = 0, f has order 1 and by Theorem 5.7.12 we can find R and Ï
such that for 0 < |w âˆ’ w0 | < Ï , f takes on the value w exactly once in BR (z0 ).
Also, f takes on the value w0 only at z0 . Let U = f âˆ’1 [BÏ (w0 )] be the pre-image of

360

5 Residue Theory

BÏ (w0 ) under f . Since BÏ (w0 ) is open and f is continuous, U is open (Exercise 41,
Section 2.2) and it clearly contains z0 . So we can find an open disk Br (z0 ) that is
contained in U and BR (z0 ). Then f is one-to-one on Br (z0 ), for if f (z1 ) = f (z2 ) is a
point in BÏ (w0 ), Theorem 5.7.12 guarantees z1 = z2 . Conversely, if f  (z0 ) = 0, then
the order of f at z0 is at least 2. Applying Theorem 5.7.12 we find a neighborhood
of z0 on which f is at least two-to-one; so f is not one-to-one in this case.

A few comments are in order regarding the inverse function theorem. The theorem can be obtained from the classical inverse function theorem in two variables.
The latter states that the mapping (x, y) â†’ (u(x, y), v(x, y)) is one-to-one in a neighborhood of (x0 , y0 ) if its Jacobian is nonzero at (x0 , y0 ); recall that the Jacobian of
this mapping at (x, y) is


 = ux (x, y)vy (x, y) âˆ’ uy (x, y)vx (x, y).

J(x, y) = det 
vx (x, y) vy (x, y) 
Hence, using the Cauchy-Riemann equations, we find that
J(x, y) = u2x (x, y) + u2y (x, y) = | f  (x + iy)|2 .
So if f  (x0 + iy0 ) = 0, then J(x0 , y0 ) = 0 and Theorem 5.7.13 follows from the
inverse function theorem for functions of two variables as claimed.
It is important to keep in mind that the condition f  (z) = 0 for all z in Î© does not
imply that f is one-to-one on Î© . Consider f (z) = ez ; then f  (z) = ez = 0 for all z, yet
f is not one-to-one on the whole complex plane. Theorem 5.7.13 only guarantees
that f is one-to-one in some neighborhood of a point z0 where f  (z0 ) = 0. Because
this neighborhood depends on z0 in general, an obvious question is whether we can
estimate its size in terms of the sizes of f and f  . An answer to this question was
provided by the German mathematician Edmund Landau (1877â€“1938) and is known
as Landauâ€™s estimate. See Exercise 36.
The following two corollaries describe important properties of analytic functions,
which are direct applications of the local mapping theorem.
Corollary 5.7.14. (Open Mapping Property) Let Î© be a region. Then for a nonconstant analytic function f on Î© and an open subset U of Î© the set f [U] is open.
A function with this property is said to be open.
Proof. Let U be an open subset of Î© . Given w0 in f [U], let z0 be some point in U
where f (z0 ) = w0 . Since U is open, we can find a neighborhood BR (z0 ) contained in
Î© ; by applying Theorem 5.7.12 to BR (z0 ), we find that each point in the associated
BÏ (w0 ) is assumed by f . Thus BÏ (w0 ) is contained in f [U], and f [U] is open. 
Corollary 5.7.15. (Mapping of Regions) If Î© is a nonempty region (open and connected set) and f is a nonconstant analytic function on Î© , then f [Î© ] is a region.
Proof. By Corollary 5.7.14, f [Î© ] is open. To show that f [Î© ] is connected, suppose
that f [Î© ] = U âˆª V , where U and V are open and disjoint. Since f is continuous,

5.7 The Counting Theorem and RoucheÌâ€™s Theorem

361

f âˆ’1 [U] and f âˆ’1 [V ] are open (note that since f is defined on Î© , when taking a preimage, we only consider those points in Î© ). Clearly, f âˆ’1 [U] and f âˆ’1 [V ] are disjoint
and their union is Î© . Since Î© is connected, either Î© = f âˆ’1 [U] or Î© = f âˆ’1 [V ], and

hence f [Î© ] = U or f [Î© ] = V , implying that f [Î© ] is connected.
Another interesting application of the open mapping property of analytic functions is a simple proof of the maximum principle.
Corollary 5.7.16. (Maximum Modulus Principle) If f is analytic on a region Î© ,
such that | f | attains a maximum at some point in Î© , then f is constant in Î© .

26. Project Problem: Hurwitzâ€™s theorem. We outline a proof of a useful theorem due to the

that { fn }âˆž
n=1 is a sequence of analytic functions on a region Î© converging uniformly on every
closed and bounded subset of Î© to a function f . Then either
(i) f is identically 0 on Î© ; or
(ii) if Br (z0 ) is an open disk in Î© such that f does not vanish on Cr (z0 ), then fn and f have the
same number of zeros in Br (z0 ) for all sufficiently large n. In particular, if f is not identically 0
and f has p distinct zeros in Î© , then so do the functions fn for all sufficiently large n.
Observe that f is analytic by Theorem 4.1.10. Also, note that the theorem guarantees that, for
large n, fn and f have the same number of zeros, but these zeros are not necessarily the same for fn
and f . To see this, take fn (z) = z âˆ’ 1n and f (z) = z for all z âˆˆ Î© . Finally, observe that the possibility
that f is identically zero can arise, even if the fn â€™s are all nonzero. Simply take fn = 1n .

a closed disk such that f is nonvanishing on Cr (z0 ). Let m = min | f | on Cr (z0 ). Then m > 0 (why?).
Apply uniform convergence to get an index N such that n > N implies that | fn âˆ’ f | < m â‰¤ | f | on

Harmonic Functions and Applications
The mathematicianâ€™s patterns, like the painterâ€™s or the poetâ€™s
must be beautiful; the ideas, like the colors or the words must fit
together in a harmonious way.
-Godfrey Harold Hardy (1877â€“1947)

There are many important applications of complex analysis to real-world problems. The ones studied in this chapter are related to the fundamental differential
equation
âˆ‚ 2u âˆ‚ 2u
Î” u = 2 + 2 = 0,
âˆ‚x
âˆ‚y
known as Laplaceâ€™s equation. This partial differential equation models phenomena
in engineering and physics, such as steady-state temperature distributions, electrostatic potentials, and fluid flow, just to name a few. A real-valued function that satisfies Laplaceâ€™s equation is said to be harmonic. There is an intimate relationship
between harmonic and analytic functions. This is investigated in Section 6.1 along
with other fundamental properties of harmonic functions.
To illustrate an application, consider a twoy
dimensional plate of homogeneous material,
with insulated lateral surfaces. We represent
this plate by a region Î© in the complex plane
Plate
(see Figure 6.1). Suppose that the temperature
u
of the points on the boundary of the plate is
x
described by the function b(x, y) that does not
change with time. It is a fact of thermodynamBoundary of
u b x, y
ics that the temperature inside the plate will
eventually reach and remain at an equilibrium
distribution u(x, y), known as the steady-state
Fig. 6.1 The steady-state temperatemperature distribution, which satisfies the
ture distribution of a plate satisfies
Laplaceâ€™s equation.
equation Î” u = 0.
The Laplacian is named after the great French mathematician and physicist
Pierre-Simon de Laplace (1749â€“1827). This operator appeared for the first time
in a memoir of Laplace in 1784, in which he completely determined the attraction
of a spheroid on the points outside it. The Laplacian of a function measures the
difference between the value of the function at a point and the average value of the
function in a neighborhood of that point. Thus a function that does not vary abruptly
has a very small Laplacian. Harmonic functions have a zero Laplacian; they vary in
a very regular way. Examples of such functions include the temperature distribution
Â© Springer International Publishing AG, part of Springer Nature 2018

Undergraduate Texts in Mathematics, https://doi.org/10.1007/978-3-319-94063-2 6

in a plate, the potential of the attractive force due to a sphere, or the function that
gives the brightness of colors in an image.

Example 6.1.3. (Harmonic functions) Show that the following functions are harmonic in the stated regions.

We have seen that analytic functions have derivatives of all orders (Corollary 3.8.9). The same is true for harmonic functions. The proof of this result is
facilitated via a definition and a lemma.

Using the conjugate gradient Ï† = ux âˆ’ i uy , we can express partial derivatives of u
as the real or imaginary part of an analytic function, namely a derivative of Ï† . For example, to obtain uxxx , differentiate Ï† with respect to x twice and get Ï†  = uxxx âˆ’i uyxx .
(Note that since the derivatives of Ï† exist, we can obtain them by differentiating with
respect to any one of the variables x, y, or z). Since Ï† is analytic, all its higher-order
derivatives are analytic (Corollary 3.8.9), and so uxxx and uyxx are both harmonic by
Theorem 6.1.2. We can carry these ideas further and arrive at the following useful
result.
Corollary 6.1.9. A harmonic function defined on a region has continuous partial
derivatives of all orders.
We now discuss a result concerning the composition of a harmonic function with
an analytic one.

Harmonic Conjugates
Definition 6.1.11. Suppose that u and v are harmonic functions that satisfy the
Cauchy-Riemann equations on some open set Î© ; in other words, the function
f = u + i v is analytic in Î© . Then v is called the harmonic conjugate of u.
Can we always find a harmonic conjugate of a harmonic function u? As it turns
out, the answer depends on the function u and its domain of definition. For example,
the function ln |z| is harmonic in Î© = C \ {0} (Example 6.1.3(d)), but ln |z| has no
harmonic conjugate in that region (Exercise 34). It does, however, have a harmonic
conjugate in C \ (âˆ’âˆž, 0], namely Arg z. Our next example shows one way of using
the Cauchy-Riemann equations to find the harmonic conjugate in a region such as
the entire complex plane, a disk, or a rectangle.
Example 6.1.12. (Finding harmonic conjugates) Show that u(x, y) = x2 âˆ’ y2 + x
is harmonic in the entire plane and find a harmonic conjugate for it.

conjugate v, we use the Cauchy-Riemann equations as follows. We want u + i v to
be analytic. Hence v must satisfy the Cauchy-Riemann equations

More generally, we have the following useful result.
Proposition 6.1.13. Suppose that u is harmonic on an open set Î© and that v is a
harmonic conjugate of u on Î© . Then âˆ’u is a harmonic conjugate of v on Î© .

In Example 6.1.12, we found the harmonic conjugate of u up to an arbitrary
additive real constant. In fact, the following properties of the harmonic conjugate
are not hard to prove and are left to the reader.
Proposition 6.1.14. Suppose that u is a harmonic function in a region Î© . Then
(i) if v1 and v2 are harmonic conjugates of u in Î© , then v1 and v2 must differ by a
real constant.
(ii) If v is a harmonic conjugate of u, then v is also a harmonic conjugate of u + c
where c is a real constant.
Remark 6.1.15. Notice that in the hypothesis of Proposition 6.1.14 we are assuming
that Î© is a connected set. Otherwise the constant c in assertion (ii) may vary on each
different connected component of Î© .

Theorem 6.1.18. (Orthogonality of Level Curves) Suppose that u is a harmonic
function in a region Î© , and let v be a harmonic conjugate of u in Î© , so that f =
u + i v is analytic in Î© . Then, the two families of level curves, u(x, y) = C1 and

We now prove the maximum-minimum modulus principle for harmonic functions. Note the role of connectedness in the proof.
Theorem 6.1.20. (Maximum and Minimum Modulus Principle) Suppose that u
is a harmonic function on a region Î© . If u attains a maximum or a minimum in Î© ,
then u is constant in Î© .
Proof. By considering âˆ’u, we need only to prove the statement for maxima. We
first prove the result under the assumption that Î© is simply connected. Applying
Theorem 6.1.16 we find an analytic function f = u + i v on Î© . Consider the function
g = e f = eu eiv .
Then g is analytic in Î© and |g| = eu . Since the real exponential function is strictly
increasing, a maximum of eu corresponds to a maximum of u. By Theorem 3.9.6,
if |g| attains a maximum or a minimum in Î© , then g is constant, implying that u is
constant in Î© .
We now deal with an arbitrary region Î© . Suppose that u attains a maximum M at
a point in Î© . Let

Î©0 = {z âˆˆ Î© : u(z) < M}
Î©1 = {z âˆˆ Î© : u(z) = M}.
We have Î© = Î©0 âˆª Î©1 , Î©0 is open, and Î©1 is nonempty by assumption. It is enough
to show that Î©1 is open. By connectedness this will imply that Î© = Î©1 .
Suppose that z0 is in Î©1 , and let Br (z0 )
be an open disk in Î© centered at z0 (Figure 6.9). Since Br (z0 ) is simply connected
and the restriction of u to Br (z0 ) is a harmonic function that attains its maximum
at z0 inside Br (z0 ), it follows from the
previous case that u is constant in Br (z0 ).
Thus u(z) = M for all z in Br (z0 ), implying that Br (z0 ) is contained in Î©1 . Hence
Î©1 is open.

functions. The following corollaries of Theorem 6.1.20 are similar to already proved
results regarding the modulus of analytic functions. We relegate their proofs to the

Corollary 6.1.21. Suppose Î© is a bounded region, and u is harmonic on Î© and
continuous on the closure of Î© . Then
(i) u attains its maximum M and minimum m on the boundary of Î© ;
(ii) either u is constant or m < u < M for all points in Î© .
Corollary 6.1.22. Suppose Î© is a bounded region, and u is harmonic on Î© and
continuous on the boundary of Î© . If u is constant on the boundary of Î© , then u is
constant in Î© .
Corollary 6.1.23. Suppose Î© is a bounded region, and u1 and u2 are harmonic on
Î© and continuous on the boundary of Î© . If u1 = u2 on the boundary of Î© , then
u1 = u2 in Î© .

6.2 Dirichlet Problems
To determine the steady-state temperature distribution inside a plate Î© , we must
solve Laplaceâ€™s equation inside Î© subject to the condition that u(x, y) is a given
function b(x, y) on the boundary of Î© ; such a condition is known as a boundary condition. A problem consisting of a partial differential equation along with
specified boundary conditions is known as a boundary value problem. The spe-

380

6 Harmonic Functions and Applications

cial case involving Laplaceâ€™s equation with specified boundary values is known as a
Dirichlet problem. Solving Dirichlet problems is of paramount importance in applied mathematics, engineering, and physics. Many methods have been developed
for this purpose. The ones that we present in this section provide beautiful applications of complex analysis.

Solving and Interpreting Dirichlet Problems
An interesting example of a harmonic function is u(x, y) = a Arg z + b, which is
harmonic on C \ (âˆ’âˆž, 0] by Example 6.1.3(c) and Proposition 6.1.4. Because Arg z
is constant on rays (independent of r), it follows that u = a Arg z + b is also constant
on rays. (In fact, this is the only harmonic function with such a property. See Exercise 7.) Thus u is a good candidate for a solution of Dirichlet problems in which the
boundary data is constant on rays or independent of r. We illustrate these ideas with
an example.
Example 6.2.1. (Dirichlet problem in a quadrant)
The boundary of a very large sheet
of metal (thought of as the quarter
plane Î© ) is kept at the constant temperatures 100â—¦ on the bottom and
50â—¦ on the left, as illustrated in Figure 6.10. After a long enough period

In Example 6.2.2 we used a harmonic function that was independent of x. Similarly, we can find harmonic functions that are independent of y (hence uy = 0 and

Harmonic Conjugates, Isotherms, and Heat Flow
In Example 6.2.1, the temperature of the boundary is kept at two constant values,
100â—¦ and 50â—¦ . Our physical intuition tells us that, as the plate is insulated, the temperature values of the points inside the plate vary between these two values and equal
those values only at the boundary. It is natural to ask for those points (x, y) inside
the plate with the same temperature u(x, y) = T , where 50 < T < 100. These points
lie on curves inside the plate, called curves of constant temperature or isotherms.
Isotherms have many practical applications. Computing them leads to interesting
properties of harmonic functions.

Related to the topic of isotherms is the topic of curves of heat flow. These are the
curves along which the heat is flowing inside the plate. To determine these curves,
we use Fourierâ€™s law of heat conduction, which states that heat flows from hot to
cold in the direction in which the temperature difference is the greatest. Clearly the
change in temperature is greatest along directions moving away from the isotherms,
i.e., along curves perpendicular to them. Hence the curves of heat flow are orthogonal to the isotherms.
Recall from vector calculus that the gradient vector âˆ‡u = (ux , uy ) points in
the direction of greatest change in a function. The gradient is perpendicular to the
level curves of u(x, y). Fourierâ€™s law states that heat flows along âˆ’âˆ‡u, and thus
curves of heat flow are orthogonal to level curves of u, and hence coincident with
level curves of v. So to find the curves of heat flow in a plate, it is enough to find a
harmonic conjugate v(x, y) of the steady-state temperature distribution u(x, y), since
by Theorem 6.1.18 the level curves of v are orthogonal to the level curves of u.

384

6 Harmonic Functions and Applications
