So far we have used our knowledge of analytic functions to solve Dirichlet problems by guessing the solution. Guessing is certainly a legitimate method that can
be used in solving differential equations and computing indefinite integrals. Further development of the theory of analytic and harmonic functions will be necessary
to tackle more general Dirichlet problems. Topics such as the Poisson integral formula, Fourier series, and conformal mappings are examples of theories and tools
that provide us with systematic ways for solving Dirichlet problems.
Other issues that could be addressed concern the uniqueness of the solution of a
Dirichlet problem. Consider the problem in Example 6.2.1, whose solution is
u(x, y) = âˆ’

100 âˆ’1  y 
+ 100.
tan
Ï€
x

If we add to this solution the harmonic function Ï† (x, y) = xy, we obtain the harmonic function Ïˆ (x, y) = u(x, y) + xy, which solves Laplaceâ€™s equation. Moreover,
because xy vanishes on the boundary of the first quadrant, Ïˆ and u have the same

6.2 Dirichlet Problems

385

boundary values. Thus, Ïˆ is another solution of the Dirichlet problem in Example 6.2.1. This situation is counterintuitive since we would like to think of the solution as representing a temperature distribution and as such it must be unique. As it
turns out, on unbounded regions, such as the one in Example 6.2.1, if we require the
(natural) condition that the temperature distribution be bounded, then the solution
will be unique. For example, with this additional boundedness assumption, we can
no longer add the function xy to the solution in Example 6.2.1 to obtain another
solution.

Example 6.3.2. (A steady-state problem on a disk)
The temperature on the boundary of a circular plate of radius 1 with insulated lateral surface and center placed at the origin
is a function of the radial angle Î¸ which
varies between 0â—¦ and 100â—¦ according to

Fig. 6.24 The boundary function, as a function of Î¸ .

Solution. (a) To find the steady-state temperature, we must solve the Dirichlet
problem with boundary data f . According to (6.3.4), the solution is u(r, Î¸ ) =
50 âˆ’ 50 r cos Î¸ , 0 â‰¤ r < 1. This function is harmonic and equals f (Î¸ ) when r = 1
(Figure 6.25).
(b) Since the temperature on the boundary varies between 0 and 100, by the
maximum-minimum principle for harmonic functions, the temperature inside the
plate will vary between these two limits. To find the isotherms, let 0 < T < 100 and
solve the equation u(r, Î¸ ) = T or 50 âˆ’ 50r cos Î¸ = T . Using x = r cos Î¸ , the equation
becomes 50 âˆ’ 50x = T or x = 50âˆ’T
50 . Thus the isotherms are the intersections with
the unit disk of the vertical lines x = 50âˆ’T
50 . The isotherms vary between x = 1 and
x = âˆ’1 as T varies between 0 and 100. In view of Theorem 6.1.18 the heat flows
along the curves that are orthogonal to the isotherms. In this case, it is not difficult
to verify that heat flows inside the unit disk in the direction of the horizontal lines
y = b (Figure 6.26).

6 Harmonic Functions and Applications

In the next section, we show that the solution of the Dirichlet problem with arbitrary piecewise continuous boundary data f can be expressed in the form (6.3.4) if
we allow the series to have infinitely many terms. The series we thus obtain is called
Fourier series.

Chapter 7

Conformal Mappings
First, it is necessary to study the facts, to multiply the number
of observations, and then later to search for formulas that connect them so as thus to discern the particular laws governing
a certain class of phenomena. In general, it is not until after
these particular laws have been established that one can expect
to discover and articulate the more general laws that complete
theories by bringing a multitude of apparently very diverse phenomena together under a single governing principle
-Augustin-Louis Cauchy (1789â€“1857)

This chapter presents a sampling of successful applications of complex analysis in applied mathematics, engineering, and physics. After laying down the theory
and methods of conformal mappings we discuss Dirichlet problems; in particular,
we derive Poissonâ€™s integral formula in the upper half-plane and other regions, by
performing a suitable change of variables. In Section 7.4, we broaden the scope
of our applications with the Schwarz-Christoffel transformation, which is a method
for finding conformal mappings of regions bounded by polygonal paths. The section
contains interesting applications from fluid flow.
In Section 7.5, we derive a Poisson integral formula for simply connected regions
in terms of the so-called Greenâ€™s function. The solution is based on a simple change
of variables and the mean value property of harmonic functions. This approach illustrates in a natural way the applicability of Greenâ€™s functions to the solution of
boundary value problems.
In Section 7.6, we tackle other famous boundary value problems, including
Laplaceâ€™s equation and Poissonâ€™s equation. We follow the approach of changing
variables using conformal mappings, and we derive general formulas for the solutions in terms of Greenâ€™s functions and the Neumann function. Both functions are
computed explicitly in important special cases.

7.1 Basic Properties
In this section we present some basic mapping properties of analytic functions.
These properties will be useful in the solutions of partial differential equations in2
2
volving the Laplacian Î” u = âˆ‚âˆ‚ xu2 + âˆ‚âˆ‚ yu2 . We start with a review from calculus of the
notion of tangent lines to curves in parametric form. We state these results using the
convenient complex notation.

7 Conformal Mappings

Suppose that Î³ is a smooth path parametrized by z(t) = x(t) + i y(t), a â‰¤ t â‰¤ b.
Assume that z (t) = x (t) + iy (t) = 0 for all t, which guarantees the existence of
a tangent. The tangent line to the curve at z0 = z(t) has direction of z (t); we can
characterize this direction by specifying arg z (t) (see Figure 7.1). Thus the direction
of the tangent line at a point on a path z(t) is determined by the argument of z (t).

Let z0 be a point in the z-plane, let Î³1 and Î³2 be two smooth paths that intersect at
z0 , and let L1 and L2 denote the tangent lines to Î³1 and Î³2 at z0 . We say that Î³1 and Î³2

Fig. 7.3 A linear mapping f (z) = az + b (a = 0) rotates by an angle
arg a, dilates by a factor |a|, and translates by b. In particular, it preserves

To explain the geometric meaning of the mapping properties discussed in this
section, let us consider the simple example of a linear mapping f (z) = az + b, where
a = 0 and b are complex numbers. As usual, we consider a mapping as taking points
in the z-plane to points in the w-plane. Using our geometric interpretation of addition and multiplication of complex numbers, we see that the effect of the linear
mapping f (z) = az + b is to rotate by the fixed angle arg a, dilate by the factor |a|,
and then translate by b. (Note that the rotation and dilation commute, so it does not
matter which one we apply first. But we cannot change the order of the translation;

7.1 Basic Properties

405

it comes last.) In particular, if Î³1 and Î³2 are two smooth paths that intersect at angle Î± at z0 , then their images under f are two paths in the w-plane that intersect at
w0 = f (z0 ); since f (z) has rotated each curve by the same angle arg a, the angle of
their intersection in the w-plane is still Î± . Furthermore, this mapping preserves the
orientation of Î³1 (as being either clockwise or counterclockwise) with respect to Î³2
(Figure 7.3). With this example in mind, we introduce the following definition.
Definition 7.1.1. A mapping defined in a neighborhood of a point z0 is said to be
conformal at z0 if it preserves the magnitude and the orientation of the angles between curves intersecting at z0 .
Now suppose that f is analytic at z0 and f  (z0 ) = 0. Then f is defined and is
analytic in a neighborhood of z0 , and from the study of the derivative in Section 2.3
we know that f is approximately linear in a neighborhood of z0 . More precisely, for
z near z0 we have
f (z) = f (z0 ) + f  (z0 )(z âˆ’ z0 ) + Îµ (z)(z âˆ’ z0 ),
where Îµ (z) â†’ 0 as z â†’ z0 . So, near z0 , we can write f (z) â‰ˆ f  (z0 )z + b, where
b = âˆ’ f  (z0 )z0 + f (z0 ) is a constant. From what we just discovered about linear
mappings, this suggests that f is conformal at z0 ; it rotates by an angle Î¸ = arg f  (z0 )
and scales by a factor | f  (z0 )|. Indeed, we have the following important result.
Theorem 7.1.2. (Conformal Property) Suppose that f is analytic in a neighborhood of a point z0 in C and suppose that f  (z0 ) = 0. Then f is conformal at z0 .
Proof. Let Î³ be a smooth path through z0 , parametrized by Î³ (t) = x(t) + i y(t), with
Î³ (t0 ) = z0 and Î³  (t0 ) = 0. Then the image of Î³ by f is a path parametrized by f (Î³ (t))
that passes through w0 = f (z0 ) in the w-plane. Since Î³  (t0 ) = 0, the direction

Boundary Behavior
We use conformal mappings to transform boundary value problems consisting of
Laplaceâ€™s equation along with boundary conditions. To handle the effect of the
mapping on the boundary conditions, it would be nice to know that the boundary
is mapped to the boundary. But as the following simple example shows, this may
fail in general.

The condition f  (z) = 0 for all z in a region Î© ensures only that f is one-toone locally, and not necessarily one-to-one on the entire region; see Theorem 5.7.13
(Inverse Function Theorem) and Example 7.1.3. We show below that if f is analytic
and one-to-one, then it maps the boundary of its domain to the boundary of the
range. Before we do so, let us clarify certain issues. We know from Corollary 5.7.13
(Mapping of Regions) that if f is analytic and nonconstant on a region Î© , then f [Î© ]
is a region. So all the points in Î© are mapped to the open connected set f [Î© ]. Now
f might not be defined on the boundary of Î© , so we need a special definition to
describe how f maps the boundary of Î© .

on a region Î© . Then f maps the boundary of Î© onto the boundary of f [Î© ].

If the conformal mapping can be extended to a continuous function on the boundary, the following version of Theorem 7.1.5 will be useful.
Corollary 7.1.6. Suppose that f is analytic and one-to-one in a region Î© . Suppose
that f extends continuously to the closure Î© . Then for any point Î± of the boundary
of Î© the point f (Î± ) lies on the boundary of f [Î© ].
Proof. Let {zn }âˆž

an interior point of Î© . Thus f (Î± ) is a boundary point of f [Î© ].

7 Conformal Mappings

mapping. Moreover, from Section 5.7, we know that the inverse function exists on
Î©  = f [Î© ] and is analytic and one-to-one. In this situation, we say that Î© and Î© 
are conformally equivalent regions.
The following famous theorem of Riemann states that any simply connected region of the complex plane other than the plane itself is conformally equivalent to
the open unit disk.
Theorem 7.1.9. (Riemann Mapping Theorem) Let Î© be a simply connected region in the complex plane other than the complex plane itself. Then there is a oneto-one analytic function f that maps Î© onto the unit disk |w| < 1. The mapping f is
unique if we specify that f (z0 ) = 0 and f  (z0 ) > 0, for some z0 in Î© .
The proof of the Riemann mapping theorem extends beyond the scope of this
book. A proof of this theorem can be found in L. Ahlfors: Complex Analysis, 3rd
Ed., Inter. Ser. in Pure & Applied Math. McGraw-Hill Ed, 1979 or in J. Conway:
Functions of One Complex Variable I, GTM 11, 2nd Ed., Springer 1978.
In the next section we pursue the construction of important conformal mappings.
Some of these are then used to solve Dirichlet problems in general simply connected
domains by transforming the Dirichlet problem to the unit disk. This is achieved in
Section 7.3.

7.2 Linear Fractional Transformations
Our success in solving boundary value problems involving Laplaceâ€™s equation is
closely tied to our ability to construct conformal mappings between regions in the
plane. A good place to start our study of special conformal mappings is on the unit
disk, since there we have a general formula for the solution of the Dirichlet problem,
namely the Poisson integral formula. As we will soon see, the most suitable mappings for regions involving disks and lines are the linear fractional transformations:

The previous examples illustrate how a linear fractional transformation can be

useful formula.

before replacing z by z3 and w by w3 .) The uniqueness part is delegated to Exercises 9â€“10.

(b) Of course we can determine the angle between C1 and C2 by finding their equations, then the slopes of the tangent lines at âˆ’2, and then the angle between the
tangent lines. A better way is to calculate the angle between the images of C1 and

on C2 . We have Ï† ( 23 i) = i. Thus Ï† maps C2 onto the v-axis. Since Ï† is conformal
at z = âˆ’2, it preserves the angles at this point. Thus, the angle between the circles
at z = âˆ’2 is equal to the angle between their images, the u- and v-axes, which is

clearly Ï€2 .
Some applications concerning the electrostatic potential inside a capacitor formed
by two cylinders lead to Dirichlet problems in regions bounded by two circles in the
plane, which are not necessarily concentric. The problems are easier to solve when
the two circles are concentric, giving rise to an annular region. Thus there is a great
advantage in using a conformal mapping to center the circles before solving the
Dirichlet problem. In what follows, we use a specific example to illustrate this process. More general examples are presented in the exercises.

Composing Elementary Mappings
Our next goal is to compose elementary mappings studied thus far to construct nontrivial conformal mappings of regions in the plane. We provide four examples to
illustrate this process.

7.3 Solving Dirichlet Problems with Conformal Mappings
In this section we use conformal mappings to solve Dirichlet problems. Very often the difficulty in solving Dirichlet problems is due to the geometry of the region on which the problem is stated. Conformal mappings can be used to transform

430

7 Conformal Mappings

a region to one on which the ensuing boundary value problem is easier to solve.
Roughly speaking, the conformal mapping provides the change of variables that
leaves Laplaceâ€™s equation unchanged but transforms the boundary conditions. At
the heart of the method lie the Riemann mapping theorem (Theorem 7.1.9) and the
invariance of Laplaceâ€™s equation by conformal mappings [identity 6.1.3].
To make things precise, let Î© be a simply connected region on the plane. Suppose
we have the Dirichlet problem

Î” u = 0 on Î©

u = b on âˆ‚ Î© .

(7.3.1)

We fix a conformal map f from Î© onto another domain W = f [Î© ]. Then we consider the new Dirichlet problem

Î” U = 0 on W,

U = b â—¦ f âˆ’1 on âˆ‚ W.

(7.3.2)

Let U be a solution of the Dirichlet problem (7.3.2) on W . We claim that the function
u = U â—¦ f solves the Dirichlet problem (7.3.1) on Î© . Indeed, the conformal mapping
transfers one boundary function into the other. Moreover, identity (6.1.3) yields

Î” u = Î” (U â—¦ f ) = | f  |2 Î” U
and since f  is never vanishing, it follows that Î” u = 0 on Î© if and only if Î” U = 0 on
W . This method is illustrated in Figure 7.39 when W is the unit disk D. In practice
W could also be the upper half space or any other domain on which we know how
to solve a Dirichlet problem.

z-plane

Fig. 7.39 A one-to-one conformal mapping f of Î© onto the unit
disk D = f [Î© ] takes boundary to boundary and preserves Laplaceâ€™s
equation. It transforms the conditions Î” u = 0 on Î© and u = b on
âˆ‚ Î© to Î” U = 0 on D and U = b â—¦ f âˆ’1 on âˆ‚ D. The solution of the
initial Dirichlet problem in Î© is then u(z) = U( f (z)).

This method turns out to be phenomenally powerful. Not only we will be able to
solve specific problems, but we can also take general formulas, such as the Poisson
integral formula on a disk, and produce similar formulas for the solution of Dirichlet
problems on new regions in the plane.
Recall that for Dirichlet problems where the boundary data is constant along
rays, we can find a solution using a branch of the argument. We denote by argÎ± z
the branch of the argument with a branch cut at angle Î± , and by Arg z the principal

7.3 Solving Dirichlet Problems with Conformal Mappings

431

branch with a branch cut along the negative real axis. These functions, being the
imaginary parts of the corresponding logarithm branches, are harmonic everywhere
except on their branch cuts.
Recall also that a linear combination of harmonic functions with real scalars is
again a harmonic function (Proposition 6.1.4). For example, u(z) = 100
Ï€ (Ï€ âˆ’ Arg z)
is harmonic in the upper half-plane with boundary values u(x) = 100 if x > 0 and
u(x) = 0 if x < 0. This solution to a very simple Dirichlet problem in the upper
half-plane helps us solve a somewhat difficult Dirichlet problem on the unit disk.
Example 7.3.1. (Steady-state temperature distribution in a disk) The boundary
of a circular plate of unit radius with insulated lateral surface is kept at a fixed
temperature distribution equal to 100â—¦ on the upper semicircle and 0â—¦ on the lower
semicircle (see Figure 7.40). Find the steady-state temperature inside the plate.
Solution. To answer this question, we must solve Î” u = 0 inside the unit disk with
boundary values u = 100 on the upper semicircle and u = 0 on the lower semicircle.
While the geometry of the circle makes it difficult to understand the effect of the
boundary conditions on the solution inside the unit disk, the corresponding boundary value problem in the upper half-plane has a simple solution. To transform the
original problem into a problem in the upper half-plane, we use the linear fractional
transformation Ï† (z) = i 1âˆ’z
1+z from Example 7.2.3(ii). It is easy to verify that Ï† takes
the upper semicircle onto the positive real axis, and the lower semicircle onto the
negative real axis. Thus Ï† transforms the given Dirichlet problem into a Dirichlet
problem in the upper half-plane with boundary values shown in Figure 7.41.

Suppose that Î© is a region, f1 and f2 are two functions defined on the boundary
of Î© , and u1 and u2 are solutions of the Dirichlet problems on Î© with boundary values f1 and f2 , respectively. Since Laplaceâ€™s equation Î” u = 0 is linear, it is straightforward to check that u = u1 + u2 solves the Dirichlet problem on Î© with boundary
values u = f1 + f2 on the boundary of Î© . This useful process of generating a solution by adding solutions of two or more related problems is called superposition of
solutions. It will appear again in our study of other linear equations.

7.4 The Schwarz-Christoffel Transformation
In this section we describe a method for constructing one-to-one analytic mappings
of the upper half-plane onto polygonal regions. We start by setting the notation.
Suppose that Î© is a region in the w-plane, bounded by a positively oriented
polygonal path P with n sides. Let w1 , w2 , . . . , wn denote the vertices of P, counted
consecutively as we trace the path through its positive orientation; see Figure 7.74

444

7 Conformal Mappings

when n = 5. If P is bounded, then the point wn is taken to be the initial and terminal
point of the closed path P. If P is unbounded, we take wn = âˆž and think of P as a
polygon with n âˆ’ 1 vertices w1 , w2 , . . . , wnâˆ’1 (Figure 7.75). It will be convenient to
measure the exterior angle at a vertex, and so we let Î¸ j denote the angle that we make
as we turn the corner of the polygon at w j . We choose 0 < |Î¸ j | < Ï€ ( j = 1, . . . , n); a
positive value corresponds to a left turn, and a negative value corresponds to a right
turn. In Figure 7.74, Î¸2 is negative while all other Î¸ j are positive.

polygonal boundary with corner angles measured from the
outside.

region with n sides (n = 4) and
n âˆ’ 1 vertices.

In the case of a bounded polygon, this line segment will connect back to wn (Exercise 11). The polygon is then closed; for closed polygons, after turning the last
corner our combined angle of turns is Î¸1 + Î¸2 + Â· Â· Â· + Î¸n = 2Ï€ . Thus we have shown
that the mapping whose derivative is given by (7.4.1) takes the real line onto a polygon with vertices w j = f (x j ) and exterior angles Î¸ j . Since the upper half-plane is to
our left as we traverse the real line rightward, conformality ensures that the image
region is to our left as we trace P in the positive sense (i.e., f maps the upper halfplane onto the interior of P). The converse of these statements is also true, although
we will not prove it. That is, for every polygonal path P with vertices w j and exterior angles Î¸ j , it can be shown that we can find ordered real numbers x1 , . . . , xnâˆ’1 ,
and complex numbers A and B such that the mapping in (7.4.2) whose derivative is
given by (7.4.1) takes the real line onto P. Moreover, two of the x j â€™s can be chosen
arbitrarily.

Image of Level Curves
Suppose that f is a Schwarz-Christoffel transformation taking the upper half-plane
onto a region Î© in the w-plane. If we want to solve a Dirichlet problem Î” U(w) = 0,
the technique is to map the problem to a corresponding problem in the upper half
of the z-plane, where a solution u of Laplaceâ€™s equation Î” u(z) = 0 with boundary
conditions can be obtained more easily. The solution in the w-plane is then U(w) =
u( f âˆ’1 (w)). However, the Schwarz-Christoffel transformation yields f , not f âˆ’1 , and
it is not always possible or easy to invert f in closed form; try for instance the
preceding examples. Nevertheless, the conformal map f is still very useful: It allows
us to find isotherms of U without actually knowing it. As we now show, this is
because the image under f of an isotherm u(z) = C (where C is a constant) is an
isotherm U(w) = C in the region Î© .
Proposition 7.4.8. (Image of Level Curves) With the preceding notation, the image
under w = f (z) of the level curve u(z) = C is a level curve U(w) = C. Thus, if Î³ (t),
a < t < b, parametrizes an isotherm of u, then f (Î³ (t)), a < t < b, parametrizes a
corresponding isotherm of U.
Proof. Since Î³ (t) is a level curve of u, u(Î³ (t)) = C. As U(w) = u( f âˆ’1 (w)), we
conclude that U( f (Î³ (t))) = u(Î³ (t)) = C, and hence f (Î³ (t)) is a level curve of U. 
This proposition is of course true for any one-to-one conformal map, not just for
Schwarz-Christoffel transformations acting on the upper half-plane. We illustrate
with an example.

452

7 Conformal Mappings

Fluid Flow
We now investigate problems in two-dimensional fluid flow, using our knowledge of
harmonic functions and the technique of conformal mappings. In an ideal situation
where fluid is flowing over a two-dimensional surface represented by an unbounded
region Î© in the z-plane, assuming that the fluid is incompressible (fixed density)
and irrotational (circulation around a closed path is zero), it can be shown that there
is a harmonic function Ï† such that the velocity V in Î© is described by the gradient

7.4 The Schwarz-Christoffel Transformation

of Ï† . That is, for (x, y) in Î© we have

Definition 7.4.10. The function Ï† is called the velocity potential of the flow. The

Ï† (x, y) = C1

are called the equipotential curves or equipotential lines. The streamlines of the

are expressed in the form

for some function Ïˆ , then, from Section 2.5, we know that we can take Ïˆ to be the

fluid will flow on the level curves of Ïˆ . If we let

Î¦ (z) = Ï† (x, y) + i Ïˆ (x, y) (z = x + i y in Î© ),

(7.4.15)

then Î¦ is analytic in Î© . It is called the complex potential of the flow.
Thus the real part of the complex potential is the velocity potential, and the level
curves of its imaginary part are the streamlines. If Î“ denotes the boundary of Î© , we
would expect the fluid to flow along Î“ or that Î“ is one of the streamlines. Thus the
points on Î“ should satisfy (7.4.14).

is called the Greenâ€™s function for the region Î© . Formula (7.5.9) is a generalized
Poisson integral formula for the simply connected region Î© .
Greenâ€™s function plays a fundamental role in the solution of important partial
differential equations (Laplaceâ€™s equation and Poissonâ€™s equation).
Like the Poisson formulas on the disk and in the upper half-plane, formula (7.5.9)
can be used to solve a general Dirichlet problem in a simply connected region Î© ,
where the boundary data is piecewise continuous. Of course, this solution depends
on the explicit formula for the conformal mapping of Î© onto the unit disk. Once
this mapping is determined, Greenâ€™s functions can be used to solve the Dirichlet
problem. We illustrate these ideas with several examples and show how we can
recapture the Poisson formulas from Greenâ€™s functions.
We often write the Greenâ€™s function G(z, Î¶ ) in terms of the real and imaginary
parts of z = x + i y and Î¶ = s + it. We also write the Greenâ€™s function using polar
coordinates of z and Î¶ , where z = reiÎ¸ and Î¶ = Ï eiÎ· .

7.6 Poissonâ€™s Equation and Neumann Problems
In this section we use Greenâ€™s functions to solve the Poisson boundary value problem on a region Î© , then apply similar techniques to solve another important type
of problem known as a Neumann problem. Suppose h is a continuous function on
the region Î© and f is a piecewise continuous function on the boundary Î“ of Î© ,
which is a closed simple curve with positive orientation. We start with the Poisson
problem, which consists of the inhomogeneous Laplace equation known as Poissonâ€™s equation along with a condition specifying the values of the function on the
boundary Î“ of Î© (Figure 7.116):

Î” u(z) = h(z) for all z in Î© ;
u(Î¶ ) = f (Î¶ ) for all Î¶ on Î“ .
This equation models, for example, the
time-independent (or steady-state) temperature distribution in a medium in the presence of heat sources. It also arises in the
study of the velocity potential of an incompressible ideal fluid flow in the presence of
sources or sinks. In this section we show
that the solution of (7.6.1)â€“(7.6.2) has a
simple expression in terms of the Greenâ€™s
function of Î© . Obviously, if h is identically
0 in Î© , (7.6.1) reduces to Laplaceâ€™s equation.

(7.6.2)

Before we solve the problem, we would like to state the following simple but
important superposition principle.
Theorem 7.6.1. (Superposition Principle) Suppose that u1 satisfies (7.6.1) in Î©
and u1 (Î¶ ) = 0 for all Î¶ on Î“ . Suppose that u2 satisfies Laplaceâ€™s equation in Î© and
u2 (Î¶ ) = f (Î¶ ) for all Î¶ on Î“ . Then u = u1 + u2 satisfies Poissonâ€™s equation (7.6.1)
with boundary conditions (7.6.2).
The proof is immediate and is omitted. Theorem 7.6.1 allows us to decompose
the general Poisson problem (7.6.1)â€“(7.6.2) into the sum of a Dirichlet problem with
boundary values f and a Poisson problem with zero boundary values as illustrated
in Figure 7.117.

Neumann Condition and Neumann Problems
When modeling heat problems in an insulated

values of the normal derivative on the boundary (Figure 7.119):

Such a problem is called a Neumann problem (after the German mathematician
Carl Gottfried Neumann (1832â€“1925)) and sometime referred to as a Dirichlet

7.6 Poissonâ€™s Equation and Neumann Problems

problem of the second kind. Condition (7.6.13) is known as a Neumann condition. The normal derivative at the boundary describes the rate of exchange of heat
with the surrounding medium or the flux of heat across the boundary. For example,
the condition f (Î¶ ) = 0 corresponds to an insulated point where there is no exchange
of heat with the surrounding medium. Since f (Î¶ ) represents the flux of heat across
the boundary of u and u represents a steady-state temperature distribution inside Î© ,
we would expect the total flux across the boundary to be zero; that is, f cannot be
arbitrary, and it has to satisfy the compatibility condition

In a Neumann problem, we are asked to find a harmonic function in terms of the
values of its normal derivative on the boundary. Such a solution is not unique, since
we can add an arbitrary constant to a solution of (7.6.12)â€“(7.6.13) and get another
solution. So now we ask: Is the solution unique up to an arbitrary constant? The
answer is affirmative. To show this, consider the difference between two solutions,
which is harmonic and has zero normal derivative on the boundary. As we now
show, a function that is harmonic and has zero normal derivative is a constant.
Theorem 7.6.7. (Uniqueness of the Neumann solution) Suppose that u is harmonic on a bounded region Î© such that âˆ‚âˆ‚ un = 0 on the boundary Î“ of Î© . Then u is
identically constant in Î© .
Proof. Suppose that u is harmonic in Î© and take u = v in Greenâ€™s first formula, so
that Î” v = 0 in Î© , and get
 



âˆ‚u
âˆ‡u Â· âˆ‡u dA = u ds = 0,
Î“ âˆ‚n

because âˆ‚âˆ‚ un = 0 on Î“ . Now the function âˆ‡u Â· âˆ‡u = (ux )2 + (uy )2 is nonnegative and
continuous on Î© . The only way for a nonnegative continuous function to integrate to
zero is to vanish identically (Lemma 3.9.5(i) proves this result in dimension one, but

476

7 Conformal Mappings

the argument also works in higher dimensions.) Hence (ux )2 + (uy )2 is identically
zero in Î© , and so ux = 0 and uy = 0 identically in Î© . Applying Theorem 2.4.7, we

see that u is constant on Î© .
In order to express the solution of the Neumann problem as an integral, motivated by Greenâ€™s function and the solution of the Dirichlet problem, we make the
following definition.
Definition 7.6.8. (Neumann Functions) Suppose that Î© is a simply connected region with boundary Î“ . Let z, Î¶ in Î© . A Neumann function for the region Î© is a
function N(z, Î¶ ) with the following properties:
(i) for each z in Î© , N(z, Î¶ ) is harmonic in Î¶ on Î© \ {z};
(ii) âˆ‚âˆ‚Nn (z, Î¶ ) = C for all z in Î© and Î¶ on Î“ ;
(iii) for each z in Î© , there is a function Î¶ â†’ u1 (z, Î¶ ) such that u1 (z, Î¶ ) is harmonic
in Î¶ on Î© , and N(z, Î¶ ) = ln |z âˆ’ Î¶ | + u1 (z, Î¶ ) for all Î¶ in Î© \ {z}.
Parts (i) and (iii) state that Neumann functions, like Greenâ€™s functions, are harmonic
inside Î© except for a singularity at z = Î¶ , which is similar to the singularity of
ln |z âˆ’ Î¶ |. Part (ii) tells us that the boundary values of the normal derivative of the
Neumann function are constant. This is the counterpart of the boundary condition
for a Greenâ€™s function, which states that a Greenâ€™s function must vanish identically
on the boundary. As we now show, the constant C in (ii) depends on the length of
the boundary Î“ .

The fact that the normal derivative of the Neumann function of an unbounded

region by using the Neumann function for the upper half-plane and a change of variables via a conformal mapping. More precisely, we have the following construction,
which is valid for unbounded regions.

Topology: Distance Between Sets
If K and S are two nonempty sets, the distance between K and S is defined to be the
smallest value of |z âˆ’ w| where z is in K and
w is in S. If a smallest value is not attained,
we define the distance to be the infimum of
all |z âˆ’ w|, where z âˆˆ K and w âˆˆ S. It is a fact
that if K is a closed and bounded subset of
C and S is a closed subset of C, and K and
S are disjoint, then the distance between K
and S is positive and is attained for a pair of
points (z0 , w0 ) in K Ã— S.

Topology: Compact Sets
A subset of the plane is compact if and only if it is bounded and closed. Compact sets are characterized by the property that every open cover has a finite subcover. Precisely, suppose that K is a
compact subsetof C and {U j } jâˆˆJ is a (possibly uncountable) family of open subsets of the plane
such that K âŠ‚ jâˆˆJ U j . Then there is a finite subset J0 of J such that
KâŠ‚



Uj ,

jâˆˆJ0

i.e., K is contained in a finite subcover of the cover {U j } jâˆˆJ .

Analysis: Uniform Continuity
A function f on a set S is called uniformly continuous if for every Îµ > 0 there is a Î´ > 0 such
that for all z1 and z2 in S, |z1 âˆ’ z2 | < Î´ â‡’ | f (z1 ) âˆ’ f (z2 )| < Îµ .
The key word in the definition of uniform continuity is â€œfor allâ€ which expresses the fact that
for a given Îµ , the choice of Î´ is the same for all points in S; in the definition of continuity at a
point, the choice of Î´ depends on the given point. Clearly, any uniformly continuous function is
continuous but the converse is not true. For instance the continuous function x2 is not uniformly
continuous on (0, âˆž).
Theorem. Every continuous function on a closed and bounded (compact) subset S of the plane is
uniformly continuous.

Analysis: The Bolzano-Weierstrass Theorem
Lemma. Every real sequence has a monotone subsequence.
Proof. To prove this, let us call a positive integer n a â€œpeakâ€ of the sequence {xn }âˆž
n=1 if m > n =â‡’
xn > xm . If the sequence {xn }âˆž
n=1 has infinitely many peaks, n1 < n2 < n3 < Â· Â· Â· < n j < Â· Â· Â· , then the
subsequence {xn j }âˆžj=1 corresponding to these peaks is monotonically decreasing. If the sequence
{xn }âˆž
n=1 has only finitely many peaks, let N be the last peak and n1 = N + 1. Then n1 is not a
peak, since n1 > N, which implies the existence of an n2 > n1 with xn2 â‰¥ xn1 . Again, n2 > n1 > N
is not a peak; hence, there is n3 > n2 with xn3 â‰¥ xn2 . Repeating this process leads to an infinite
non-decreasing subsequence xn1 â‰¤ xn2 â‰¤ xn3 â‰¤ . . .. In either case any real sequence {xn }âˆž
n=1 has a
monotone subsequence {xn j }âˆžj=1 .
